401 of 997 DOCUMENTS


State v. Daddario


Case No. 2003CA0083


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, STARK COUNTY


2003-Ohio-6846; 2003 Ohio App. LEXIS 6168



December 15, 2003, Date of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Criminal Appeal From Stark County Court Of Common Pleas, Case 2001CR1449(B).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who pled guilty to engaging in a pattern of corrupt activity and theft from an elderly or disabled adult, among other things, appealed from the judgment of the Stark County Court of Common Pleas which overruled his motion for judicial release as being filed prematurely. Defendant filed the motion after serving 180 days of his prison sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying him judicial release under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.20 since the statute was unconstitutional. Specifically, defendant argued that the time restrictions for filing motions for judicial release found at Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.20(B)(4) violated equal protection. The appellate court held that § 2920.20(B), as applied to defendant, survived defendant's challenge. Defendant was sentenced to a total of seven years. Accordingly, defendant became eligible for judicial release after serving five years of that sentence. The appellate court held there was a rational and reasonable basis for the graduated system imposed by § 2920.20(B). The general assembly rationally and reasonably decided that offenders sentenced to prison terms of greater than five years but less than 10 years were serious enough offenders that society should be protected from them for a greater amount of time than offenders serving lesser terms of imprisonment. While his co-defendant was granted judicial release, the co-defendant was convicted of lesser degree felonies and sentenced to a shorter term of imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

403 of 997 DOCUMENTS


Sachdeva v. Conrad


No. 01AP-406


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2001-Ohio-4055; 2001 Ohio App. LEXIS 4842



November 1, 2001, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Sachdeva v. Conrad, 94 Ohio St. 3d 1487, 763 N.E.2d 1185, 2002 Ohio LEXIS 561 (2002)
Related proceeding at State ex rel. Sachdeva v. Indus. Comm'n, 2003 Ohio App. LEXIS 3428 (Ohio Ct. App., Franklin County, July 17, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] APPEAL from the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment granting defendant-appellees' motion for summary judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee filed a complaint against defendants employer, the workers' compensation commission, and its administrator requesting a judgment declaring Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4123.511(J)(4) (1999) unconstitutional as denying his right to a jury trial, under Ohio Const. art. II, § 5. The Franklin County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) granted summary judgment in favor of defendants. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that the trial court did not err in its decision granting summary judgment as § 4123.511(J)(4) was constitutional. The issue of workers' compensation fraud had to be initially decided within the commission. Since the employee's rights regarding workers' compensation benefits were governed by statute and not by common law, the employee did not have a right to a jury trial under the Ohio Constitution regarding whether he improperly received workers' compensation funds. Additionally, the type of "fraud" contemplated by former Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4123.511 was different than common law actions for fraud because the recoupment of workers' compensation funds improperly received by a claimant was controlled solely by statute pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4123.511(J)(1)-(4) (1999).


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

McGowan v. State


Case No. 2008-CA-141


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, RICHLAND COUNTY


2009-Ohio-4351; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 3681



August 25, 2009, Date of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Civil appeal from the Richland County Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 08-CV-1565.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee sex offender received a Notice of New Classification and Registration Duties based on Ohio's Adam Walsh Act, R.C. 2950.01, et seq., reclassifying him under the three-tier system, which he challenged. The Richland County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) found S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional both facially and as applied as it violated the prohibitions against both retroactive and ex post facto laws. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the trial court erred when it found that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional. The appellate court held that the trial court erred by finding that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional. S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) which amended R.C. 2950.01, et seq., was constitutional and thus, the Adam Walsh Act did not violate prohibitions against retroactive or ex post facto laws. Also, there was no violation of the right to contract pursuant to Ohio Const. art. II, § 28.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the cause was remanded for further proceedings.

 

405 of 997 DOCUMENTS


City of Cleveland v. State


No. 2009-2280


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


128 Ohio St. 3d 135; 2010-Ohio-6318; 942 N.E.2d 370; 2010 Ohio LEXIS 3322


October 12, 2010, Submitted 
December 29, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Decision reached on appeal by City of Cleveland v. State, 2011 Ohio 922, 2011 Ohio App. LEXIS 790 (Ohio Ct. App., Cuyahoga County, Mar. 3, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County, No. 92663, 185 Ohio App.3d 59, 2009 Ohio 5968, 923 N.E.2d 183.
City of Cleveland v. State, 185 Ohio App. 3d 59, 2009 Ohio 5968, 923 N.E.2d 183, 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 5017 (Ohio Ct. App., Cuyahoga County, 2009)



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State of Ohio, sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals for Cuyahoga County (Ohio), which reversed a trial court decision and granted summary judgment to appellee city. It held that R.C. 9.68 was not a general law, that it limited municipalities' home rule powers under Ohio Const. art. XVIII, § 3, and that it violated the separation of powers doctrine.


OVERVIEW: The city had enacted several ordinances regulating firearms, including Cleveland, Ohio, Codified Ordinances §§ 627.08, 627.09, 627.10, 627A.02, 628.03, and 674.05. Thereafter, the General Assembly enacted R.C. 9.68. The city filed suit, resulting in summary judgment for the State. The trial court concluded that § 9.68 was constitutional, did not violate the Home Rule Amendment, that it was a general law that was part of a comprehensive statewide legislative enactment, and that there was no violation of the single subject rule of Ohio Const. art. II, § 15(D). Further, the trial court found that the General Assembly did not abuse its legislative power. The court of appeals reversed that judgment and remanded for entry of summary judgment for the city. On further review, the court noted that the ordinances were an exercise of municipal police power and did not conflict with § 9.68. It held that under the Canton test, § 9.68 was a general law that displaced the municipal firearm ordinances, as it was part of a comprehensive statewide legislative enactment and applied uniformly across the State. Further, it did not unconstitutionally infringe on municipal home rule authority.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals, and the matter was remanded with directions for the court of appeals to address the city's assignment regarding the one subject rule.

Brumley v. State


Case No. 2008-CA-260


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, RICHLAND COUNTY


2009-Ohio-4245; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 3578



August 19, 2009, Date of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Civil appeal from the Richland County Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 08-CV-564D.
State v. Brumley, 1996 Ohio App. LEXIS 1390 (Ohio Ct. App., Portage County, Mar. 29, 1996)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sex offender challenged a notice he received that he would be reclassified for purposes of sex offender registration based on Ohio's Adam Walsh Act, R.C. 2950.01 et seq. The Richland County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) found that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional both facially and as applied because it violated the prohibitions against both retroactive and ex post facto laws. Respondent State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued on appeal that the trial court erred when it found that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional. Relying on prior holdings in similar matters, the court agreed, holding that the trial court erred by finding that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio), which amended R.C. 2950.01 et seq., was unconstitutional, either facially and as applied. The appeals court held that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) did not violate the prohibitions against retroactive or ex post facto laws and also did not violate the right to contract guaranteed by Ohio Const. art. II, § 28.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

408 of 997 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sheets


CASE NO. CA2006-04-032


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TWELFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CLERMONT COUNTY


2007-Ohio-1799; 2007 Ohio App. LEXIS 1648



April 16, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CRIMINAL APPEAL FROM CLERMONT COUNTY COURT OF COMMON PLEAS. Case No. 2004-CR-00970.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty in the Clermont County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) to one count of aggravated theft and was sentenced to four years in prison. He appealed his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's sentence was greater than the minimum authorized for his crime. The appellate court held the trial court did not violate his right to a jury trial when it sentenced him because he was not sentenced based on any additional factual findings not found by a jury, nor did he receive more than the maximum sentence authorized by the jury's findings. The Foster decision's severance of those portions of the sentencing statutes which it found to be unconstitutional was not an improper remedy. Foster did not violate due process because defendant was aware of his potential sentence when he committed his crime and Foster's remedial holding was not unexpected. The rule of lenity in R.C. § 2901.04(A) did not apply to defendant's sentencing because the statute under which he was sentenced, R.C. § 2929.14(A), was not rendered ambiguous by Foster, nor did it conflict with another statute. Defendant did not support his argument that his sentence was an abuse of discretion, so the argument was disregarded, under App. R. 12(A)(2).


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was affirmed.

City of Mayfield Heights v. Barry


NO. 82129


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


2003-Ohio-4065; 2003 Ohio App. LEXIS 3626



July 31, 2003, Date of Announcement of Decision


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by Mayfield Heights v. Barry, 100 Ohio St. 3d 1544, 2003 Ohio 6879, 800 N.E.2d 750, 2003 Ohio LEXIS 3467 (2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Criminal appeal from the Lyndhurst Municipal Court. Case Nos. 00-CRB-00545, 00-CRB-00577, 00-CRB-00578, 00-CRB-00579, 00-CRB-00580, 00-CRB-00581, 00-CRB-00582, 00-CRB-00583, 00-CRB-00584, 00-CRB-00615, 00-CRB-00616, 00-CRB-00617, 00-CRB-00618, 00-CRB-00625, 00-CRB-00626, 00-CRB-00627, 00-CRB-00628, 00-CRB-00629, 00-CRB-00630, 00-CRB-00631, 00-CRB-00643, 00-CRB-00644, 00-CRB-00645, 00-CRB-00660, 00-CRB-00661, 00-CRB-00662.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of trial court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Lyndhurst Municipal Court (Ohio) that denied his motion to dismiss and found him guilty of violating the Mayfield Heights, Ohio, Codified Ordinance § 917.14 (a) on 26 counts. On appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: In response to two citizen complaints, a building inspector for the city visited defendant's property and noticed overgrowth in his back yard, in violation of Mayfield Heights, Ohio, Codified Ordinance § 917.14 (a). The city sent a letter to defendant, instructing him to cut his grass and to eliminate the grass growing in between the stones in his front yard. According to defendant, the wet conditions in the rear of his yard made it impossible to cut. Defendant received 25 citations. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss all 25 cases, raising issues of unconstitutionality and selective prosecution. The appellate court found that defendant failed to prove that the city enforced the ordinance against him arbitrarily. While he submitted evidence to demonstrate that other city residents may have been in violation of the ordinance, he failed to prove that those residents were the subject of a citizen complaint, upon which the city thereafter failed to respond. Further, defendant failed to demonstrate that the city enforced the ordinance against him in an arbitrary, capricious, or discriminatory manner.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Topolosky


No. 15AP-211


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2015-Ohio-4963; 2015 Ohio App. LEXIS 4800



December 1, 2015, Rendered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL from the Franklin County Municipal Court. (M.C. No. 2014TRC-152539).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The testimony supported the trial court's conclusion that the State proved substantial compliance with testing procedures in Ohio Admin. Code 3701-53-06 because the forensic scientist testified that she followed the laboratory manual for the state highway patrol crime lab when analyzing defendant's urine sample, which was a simple statement of fact made by a witness which the trial court was free to accept or reject; [2]-Defendant presented no evidence regarding the impact of sodium fluoride on the accuracy of analysis of a urine sample for drugs or alcohol. The presence or absence of that additive, absent evidence of its adulterating effect, did not conclusively preclude use of the resulting analysis; [3]-R.C. 4511.19(A)(1)(j)(viii)(II) was not void for vagueness and did not violate constitutional guarantees of equal protection and due process.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

415 of 997 DOCUMENTS


McClanahan v. Ohio Pub. Emples. Ret. Sys.


No. 05AP-1080


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2006-Ohio-5867; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 5805



November 7, 2006, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by McClanahan v. Ohio Pub. Emp. Ret. Sys., 2007 Ohio 1266, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 775 (Ohio, Mar. 28, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL from the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas. (C.P.C. No. 04CVH03-3518).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife filed a declaratory judgment action against defendant, the Ohio Public Employees Retirement System (PERS), seeking a declaration that R.C. § 145.46(E) was unconstitutional and that she was entitled to payment of the decedent's retirement benefits. A magistrate found that the wife was not entitled to the requested relief. The Franklin County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) adopted the magistrate's decision. The wife appealed.


OVERVIEW: The wife was denied benefits because the decedent died before the effective date of his retirement plan change to a joint survivorship annuity. The appellate court held that the wife did not overcome the presumptive constitutionality of R.C. § 145.46(E) as she failed to demonstrate that the statute violated her right to equal protection. Thus, the trial court properly determined that she was not entitled to the requested declaratory relief. The wife did not demonstrate that she was unconstitutionally treated differently than similarly situated persons. She was a widow of a PERS retiree, but she argued that she was unconstitutionally treated differently than widows of Police and Fire Pension Fund retirees, even though she conceded that the existence of separate retirement systems in Ohio was rational and constitutional. Thus, in the absence of evidence demonstrating that members of PERS and the Police and Fire Pension Fund, or their beneficiaries, were similarly situated, the wife was not constitutionally entitled to be treated the same, in regard to when a member's retirement plan change was effective, as widows of retirees under the Police and Fire Pension Fund.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

416 of 997 DOCUMENTS


W. Chester Twp. Zoning v. Fromm


CASE NO. CA2000-12-253


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TWELFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, BUTLER COUNTY


145 Ohio App. 3d 172; 762 N.E.2d 400; 2001 Ohio App. LEXIS 3454



August 6, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of the judgment of the Butler County Area III Court (Ohio) which convicted her of building a fence on her property in violation of the West Chester Township, Ohio, Zoning Resolution (the zoning resolution) §§ 5.02, 11.08.


OVERVIEW: The property owner had a stockade fence built on her property. The fence was comprised of 24 posts set in concrete, 1104 pickets arranged vertically and held in place by 3 horizontal boards, which in turn were bolted to the posts. The side of the fence showing the supporting posts faced the neighbor's property. By complaint, the property owner was charged with building a privacy fence with its unfinished portion facing adjacent properties. The appellate court held the zoning resolution was not a criminal ordinance and was constitutional. It held the zoning resolution's use of the word "unfinished" referred to the supporting posts showing outward. It also held the township did not exceed its statutory authority in enacting the zoning resolution.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

417 of 997 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ware


No. 96327


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


2011-Ohio-5665; 2011 Ohio App. LEXIS 4626



November 3, 2011, Released and Journalized


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Criminal Appeal from the Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas. Case No. CR-543201.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's motion to suppress was denied. The Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) convicted defendant of failure to comply with the order or signal of a police officer, in violation of R.C. 2921.331(B). he was sentenced to four months of community control sanctions. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by denying his motion to suppress. The appellate court held that the trial court did not err in denying the motion to suppress because the officers did not abridge the protections guaranteed by the Fourth Amendment. Faced with a potentially violent and unpredictable situation late at night, coupled with the driver's physical resistance to a pat down and defendant's simultaneous furtive movements in the vehicle believed to be connected to the crime, the officers had articulable grounds to suspect criminal activity. The officers testified that defendant's furtive movements were consistent with an individual reaching for a weapon. Further, R.C. 2921.331(B) was constitutionally applied to defendant because the vehicle was moving. The officer's discharge of his weapon constituted a secondary visual signal for defendant to stop the operation of the vehicle at the time that he was in fact moving the vehicle. Finally, the conviction was not against the manifest weight of the evidence because defendant disobeyed the officer's order to stop and accelerated the car away from the officers.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

418 of 997 DOCUMENTS



Pataskala Banking Co. v. Etna Twp. Bd. of Zoning Appeals


Case Nos. 08 CA 128, 129 and 130


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, LICKING COUNTY


2009-Ohio-3108; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 2686



June 25, 2009, Date of Judgment Entry


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by Pataskala Banking Co. v. Etna Twp. Bd. of Zoning Appeals, 2009 Ohio 6015, 2009 Ohio 6015, 2009 Ohio LEXIS 3251 (Ohio, Nov. 18, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Civil Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas, Case Nos. 06 CV 1870, 1871 & 1872.
Pataskala Banking Co. v. Etna Twp. Bd. of Zoning Appeals, 2008 Ohio 2770, 2008 Ohio App. LEXIS 2330 (Ohio Ct. App., Licking County, June 9, 2008)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bank sought judicial review, under R.C. Chapter 2506, in the Licking County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) of the decisions of appellee township board of zoning appeals (board) denying the bank's appeal of a stop work order and its variance request. The trial court, on remand, affirmed the board's decisions, and the bank appealed.


OVERVIEW: After the county planning commission gave the bank a variance to build a driveway on an adjacent open space parcel, the township told the bank to stop building it. The appellate court held res judicata did not bar the township's decision that the driveway violated zoning rules because the variance concerned traffic safety, while the zoning restriction regulated driveways over open space, so nothing decided by the planning commission was relitigated. It was no abuse of discretion to find the open space was an enforceable area of a planned unit development because the township's acceptance of a preliminary plan supported this finding. It was no abuse of discretion to affirm the board's denial of the bank's variance request because Etna Township, Ohio, Zoning Res. art. 2 barred variances allowing establishment of a prohibited use. The board did not act unconstitutionally because (1) Etna Township, Ohio, Zoning Res. art. 2, § 300 was not vague in requiring a zoning permit for the driveway, and (2) a claim that other "reserve" areas in the adjacent subdivision might allow vehicular access did not show an equal protection violation, as they were not similarly situated to the parcel.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


 

419 of 997 DOCUMENTS


State v. Adams


CASE NO. 12 MA 26


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, MAHONING COUNTY


2013-Ohio-1433; 2013 Ohio App. LEXIS 1334



March 25, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by State v. Adams, 136 Ohio St. 3d 1473, 2013-Ohio-3790, 993 N.E.2d 778, 2013 Ohio LEXIS 1947 (2013)
Subsequent appeal at, Decision reached on appeal by State v. Adams, 2014 Ohio App. LEXIS 638 (Ohio Ct. App., Mahoning County, Feb. 21, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Criminal Appeal from Common Pleas Court, Case No. 00 CR 102.
State v. Adams, 2011 Ohio 6428, 2011 Ohio App. LEXIS 5285 (Ohio Ct. App., Mahoning County, Dec. 8, 2011)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two counts of attempted murder with firearm specifications. His sentence was reversed. His motion for a de novo sentencing hearing was denied. On a second remand, the Mahoning County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) advised him that, upon his release from prison, he would be subject to a mandatory term of five years of post-release control, and the consequences of violating post-release control. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of his resentencing under R.C. 2929.191. The appellate court held that the trial court's resentencing of defendant pursuant to R.C. 2929.191 was not unconstitutional. Any alleged conflict between Sup.R. 7 and R.C. 2929.191 would not have rendered the statute unconstitutional pursuant to Ohio Const. art. IV, § 5(B) and R.C. 2929.191 did not conflict with Crim.R. 32(A). Because the part of defendant's sentence improperly imposing post-release control was void, he did not have a legitimate expectation of finality in his sentence and thus, his resentencing did not violate the Due Process or Double Jeopardy clauses. Finally, the trial court's application of R.C. 2929.191 did not violate the Ex Post Facto Clause because the statutory sentence-correction mechanism was in effect at the time the trial court conducted the de novo resentencing pursuant to a Foster remand. Additionally, because the indictment, sentencing entries, and other pleadings contained a date-stamp by the Mahoning County Clerk of Courts, the trial court had subject matter jurisdiction over the case.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

420 of 997 DOCUMENTS



Fleming v. AAS Serv.


CASE NO. 2007-P-0071


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, ELEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, PORTAGE COUNTY


177 Ohio App. 3d 778; 2008-Ohio-3908; 896 N.E.2d 175; 2008 Ohio App. LEXIS 3295



August 1, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal allowed by Fleming v. AAS Serv., 120 Ohio St. 3d 1486, 2009 Ohio 278, 900 N.E.2d 197, 2009 Ohio LEXIS 99 (2009)
Reversed by Fleming v. A.A.S. Serv., 2010 Ohio 3132, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 1641 (Ohio, July 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:
Civil Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 2006 CV 00974.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a worker and his spouse, appealed from a decision of the Portage County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellees, the owners of a home where the worker was cleaning a chimney and the worker's employer. The worker's action asserted negligence against the owners and an intentional tort against the employer.


OVERVIEW: The worker was on the roof of the owners' home when a bucket started to slide off the roof. The worker ran after the bucket, and unable to stop, leapt off the roof. He sustained injuries in the fall. Summary judgment in favor of the owners was proper. Appellants failed to establish that the owners owed any duty to the worker because there was no evidence that the owners actively participated in the work the worker did. The worker's claim that R.C. 2745.01 (2005) was unconstitutional had merit. The statute retained the same substantive standard of proof for an intentional tort against an employer that had been found unconstitutional in Brady. Pursuant to Brady, Johnson, and the supplemental analysis set forth in Kiminski, R.C. 2745.01 was unconstitutional as it violated the standards in Ohio Const. art. II, §§ 34, 35. As a result, the common law standard set fort in Fyffe was again the viable standard for intentional tort actions against an employer. Applying that standard, genuine issues of material fact existed regarding each of the three prongs of the Fyffe test such that summary judgment in favor of the employer was precluded.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the owners. The appellate court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the employer. The matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Hedrick v. State


Case No. 2008CA0127


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, RICHLAND COUNTY


2009-Ohio-3488; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 3002



July 15, 2009, Date of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 2008CV0252D.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee sex offender received a Notice of New Classification and Registration Duties based on Ohio's Adam Walsh Act (Act), R.C. 2950.01, et seq., reclassifying him as a Tier III sex offender, which he challenged. The Richland County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) found S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional both facially and as applied as it violated the prohibitions against both retroactive and ex post facto laws. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the trial court erred when it found that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional. The appellate court held that the trial court erred by finding that the Adam Walsh Act was unconstitutional. Virtually every appellate district in Ohio had upheld the Adam Walsh Act against constitutional challenges.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the cause was remanded for further proceedings.

 

422 of 997 DOCUMENTS



In re Special Docket No. 73958


Nos. 87777 and 87816


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


2008-Ohio-4444; 2008 Ohio App. LEXIS 3757; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P18,092



September 4, 2008, Released


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Civil Appeal from the Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas. Case No. SD-73958.
In re Special Docket No. 73958, 115 Ohio St. 3d 425, 2007 Ohio 5268, 875 N.E.2d 596, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 2534 (2007)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant companies sought review of an order from the Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), that found that asbestos claims asserted by appellee claimants were governed by the law as it existed prior to the effective date of Am. Sub. H.B. 292, Gen. Assem. (Ohio 2004), arising from a pretrial procedural ruling in a group of asbestos-related personal-injury cases.


OVERVIEW: The claimants alleged that their non-malignant lung diseases were proximately caused by exposure to asbestos products associated with the companies. Numerous companies sought administrative dismissal of actions in which the claimants failed to present prima facie evidence of impairment, as required by R.C. 2307.92. The trial court held that the retroactive application of H.B. 292 violated Ohio Const. art. II, § 28. On appeal after procedural wrangling, the court held that the trial court's conclusion that R.C. 2307.91-2307.93 were incompatible with the Retroactivity Clause of § 28 was error. The court noted that the statutory scheme was expressly intended to be applied retroactively, and that there was a "savings clause" under R.C. 2307.93 for use of prior law when appropriate. The court concluded that the statutory scheme was remedial or procedural, rather than substantive in nature. Further, it did not impair any vested rights of the claimants, as they only had an expectation of future benefits. Further, the law did not impose any new or additional burdens, duties, obligations, or liabilities on them. Definitions included under R.C. 2307.91 were proper for clarification purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

State v. Mihely


CASE NOS. 2001-A-0083, and 2001-A-0084


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, ELEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, ASHTABULA COUNTY


2002-Ohio-6939; 2002 Ohio App. LEXIS 6659



December 13, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Criminal Appeal from the Ashtabula County Court, Eastern Division, Case Nos. 2000 CRB 00519 and 2000 TRD 04521.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with failure to yield to a stop sign, failure to wear his safety belt, and marijuana possession. Defendant moved to suppress any and all of the evidence from an alleged unconstitutional search and seizure. The Ashtabula County Court, Eastern Division (Ohio), overruled the motion to suppress, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The arresting officer testified that he observed a vehicle approach a stop sign and that the vehicle did not make a complete stop at the sign. The officer testified that he saw that neither of the front seat passengers were wearing seat belts. Defendant agreed to a pat down search after defendant was unable to provide a driver's license, registration, or proof of insurance. The officer discovered a bag containing marijuana as a result of the search. The appellate court held that the officer had sufficient probable cause to stop the car based on his observation of traffic violations. The appellate court further held that the record demonstrated that the marijuana was immediately apparent to the officer as contraband, and therefore its subsequent removal was justified. Defendant also argued that the trial court erred in failing to rule that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2925.11 was unconstitutional when a court was required to suspend a person's driver's license for a minor misdemeanor drug offense. The appellate court held that defendant failed to rebut the presumption that the statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Certification granted by, Stay granted by Pollis v. State, 124 Ohio St. 3d 1472, 2010 Ohio 354, 921 N.E.2d 244, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 279 (2010)
Later proceeding at Pollis v. State, 124 Ohio St. 3d 1482, 2010 Ohio 486, 921 N.E.2d 653, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 415 (2010)
Discretionary appeal allowed by, Review pending at Pollis v. State, 124 Ohio St. 3d 1505, 2010 Ohio 799, 922 N.E.2d 969, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 636 (2010)
Certified question answered by, Affirmed by In re Sexual Offender Reclassification Cases, 2010 Ohio 3753, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 1931 (Ohio, Aug. 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Civil Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 2008 CV 288.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Trumbull County Court of Common Pleas, Ohio, granted summary judgment in favor of appellee State, indicating that appellant resident was properly classified as a Tier I sex offender. The resident appealed.


OVERVIEW: The resident argued that the Adam Walsh Act (Act), R.C. Chapter 2950, violated Ohio's Double Jeopardy Clause, Ohio Const. art. I, § 10. The appellate court agreed, finding that the resident had an expectation of finality in that his reporting requirements would end in 2008. However, additional punitive measures were placed on him, as he was required to comply with the new registration requirements once a year for fifteen years. He was being punished a second time for the same offense. The Act violated the Separation of Powers Doctrine because the resident had every reasonable expectation of finality in the trial court's 1998 judgment entry. The Act was an unconstitutional retroactive law, as applied to the resident. It attached new burdens and disabilities to a past transaction. The retroactive application of the Act violated the prohibition against retroactive laws in Ohio Const. art. II, § 28. The Act could not be applied to those who entered plea agreements with the State following the 1997 amendments. The resident's other arguments were rendered moot, and would not be addressed on appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded for further proceedings.

Adams v. State


Case No. 08-CA-321


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, RICHLAND COUNTY


2009-Ohio-3609; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 3086



July 21, 2009, Date Of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Appeal from the Richland County Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 08-CV-952D.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Ohio appealed a decision from the Richland County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which held that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio 2008), Ohio's Adam Walsh Act (AWA), was unconstitutional both facially and as applied to appellee inmate. The inmate was convicted of attempting unlawful sexual conduct with a minor. The matter arose in the inmate's challenge to his sexual offender reclassification.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted of the sex offense. While incarcerated, he was notified that pursuant to the Adam Walsh Act (AWA) and R.C. Chapter 2950, as amended by S.B. 10, he was reclassified as a Tier II sex offender. The inmate filed a petition to contest the reclassification, wherein he challenged the classification and the application of the AWA to him. The trial court relied on another case holding in determining that S.B. 10 was unconstitutional because it violated the prohibitions against retroactive and ex post facto laws. It further held that the application of the AWA to the inmate was barred because he was previously sentenced and classified under the law in existence at the time of his original sentence. Accordingly, judgment was entered in favor of the inmate. On appeal by the State, the court relied on judicial precedent to find that the trial court decision was in error. Rather, S.B. 10 survived constitutional challenge with respect to ex post facto, retroactive laws, and the right to contract under Ohio Const. art. II, § 28.

State v. Williams


Nos. 99-286, 99-764 and 99-765


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


88 Ohio St. 3d 513; 2000-Ohio-428; 728 N.E.2d 342; 2000 Ohio LEXIS 813


January 12, 2000, Submitted  
April 28, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 2, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 6290.
Writ of certiorari denied Suffecool v. Ohio, 531 U.S. 902, 148 L. Ed. 2d 173, 121 S. Ct. 241, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 6290 (2000)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Lake County, No. 97-L-191.

APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Portage County, No. 97-P-0059.

APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Stark County, No. 1998-CA-00101.

I. Case No. 99-286: Appellee Daniel Williams.

In May 1986, appellee Daniel Williams pleaded guilty to one count of rape in violation of R.C. 2907.02 and one count of aggravated burglary in violation of R.C. 2911.11. Williams was then sentenced to an indefinite term of seven to twenty-five years on each count, to be served concurrently. In March 1997, pursuant to R.C. Chapter 2950, authorities at the London Correctional Institution recommended a sex offender classification hearing for Williams, and that he be classified as a "sexual predator," which would subject Williams to the registration and notification provisions of R.C. Chapter 2950.

In April 1997, prior to his sex offender classification hearing and prior to his release from prison, Williams filed a motion to dismiss, alleging that R.C. Chapter 2950 is unconstitutional as applied to him. In its opinion, the trial court held that R.C. Chapter 2950 violates the Ex Post [***2] Facto Clause of the United States Constitution and the Retroactivity Clause of the Ohio Constitution with regard to any sexual offender who was sentenced prior to January 1, 1997, the effective date of R.C. Chapter 2950. The state appealed.

In September 1998, prior to the decision of the Eleventh District Court of Appeals, this court issued its opinion in State v. Cook (1998), 83 Ohio St. 3d 404, 700 N.E.2d 570, in which we held that R.C. Chapter 2950 is not a violation of either the Ex Post Facto Clause of the United States Constitution or the Retroactivity Clause of the Ohio Constitution. The court of appeals affirmed the trial court's judgment on the grounds that R.C. Chapter 2950 violates Section 1, Article I of the Ohio Constitution.

II. Case No. 99-764: Appellee Donald Worthy.

 [*3352] *4912 *347 In March 1995, appellee Donald Worthy pleaded guilty to two counts of rape in violation of R.C. 2907.02, and one count of gross sexual imposition in violation of R.C. 2907.05. The counts to which Worthy pleaded guilty involved child victims who were all under the age of thirteen. Worthy was sentenced to an indefinite term of ten to twenty-five [***3] years on each count of rape, and one year on the gross sexual imposition count, with all sentences running concurrently.

In April 1997, the Ohio Department of Rehabilitation and Correction recommended that Worthy be adjudicated a "sexual predator" pursuant to R.C. Chapter 2950. Prior to his sex offender classification hearing and his release from prison, Worthy filed a motion to dismiss the recommendation that he be declared a "sexual predator." The trial court granted the motion to dismiss, holding that R.C. Chapter 2950 violates the Ex Post Facto Clause of the United States Constitution and the Retroactivity Clause of the Ohio Constitution. The state filed a timely appeal. The court of appeals, relying on State v. Williams, 1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 217 (Jan. 29, 1999), Lake App. No. 97-L-191, unreported, 1999 WL 76633, affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

III. Case No. 99-765: Appellant Paul D. Suffecool.

In January 1983, appellant Paul D. Suffecool pleaded guilty to one count of statutory rape in violation of R.C. 2907.02, for raping a child under the age of thirteen. The trial court sentenced Suffecool to an indeterminate term of incarceration of seven to twenty-five [***4] years. Suffecool also had prior convictions for the rape of a nineteen-year-old girl, and for the kidnapping of two teenagers.

In March 1998, prior to Suffecool's release from prison, a sex offender classification hearing was conducted pursuant to R.C. Chapter 2950. At the hearing, the state introduced evidence relating to Suffecool's convictions, a psychiatric evaluation, and statements made to police in which Suffecool admitted to having uncontrollable sexual urges for young girls. Defense counsel introduced evidence that Suffecool had participated in numerous counseling programs while in prison. The trial court adjudicated Suffecool as a "sexual predator."

Suffecool appealed his sexual predator classification to the Fifth District Court of Appeals. Suffecool argued that R.C. Chapter 2950, inter alia, violates the Double Jeopardy Clause of the United States and Ohio Constitutions, and that R.C. Chapter 2950 is unconstitutionally vague. The court of appeals held that R.C. Chapter 2950 is constitutional and affirmed the trial court's decision.

These cases are now before this court pursuant to the allowance of discretionary appeals.
State v. Worthy, 1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 677 (Ohio Ct. App., Portage County, Feb. 26, 1999)
State v. Suffecool, 1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 1517 (Ohio Ct. App., Stark County, Mar. 8, 1999)
State v. Williams, 1999 Ohio App. LEXIS 217 (Ohio Ct. App., Lake County, Jan. 29, 1999)



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed and causes remanded [***5] in case Nos. 99-286 and 99-764. Judgment affirmed in case No. 99-765.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated proceedings, the State, pursuant to the allowance of discretionary appeals, sought a determination regarding the constitutionality of the sex offender statute, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. ch. 2950 (1997).


OVERVIEW: The court was asked to determine whether Ohio Rev. Code Ann. ch. 2950 (1997) was unconstitutional. In two cases, the appellate court had reversed the convictions, holding that ch. 2950 was unconstitutional while, in the third case, the conviction was affirmed. After reviewing other states' sex offender registration laws as well as the legislation history, the court held that ch. 2950 was constitutional, neither violating the prohibitions again double jeopardy, bills of attainder or equal protection under both the federal and state constitutions, nor did it violate citizen's rights under Ohio Const. art. I, § 1. Although it impacted the lives of convicted sex offenders, ch. 2950 addressed legitimate governmental interests without a detrimental effect on individual constitutional rights. It did not violate a convicted sex offender's right of privacy.


OUTCOME: The court held that the statute was constitutional on the grounds raised in the appeals. It reversed the decisions of the courts of two appeals, remanding to the trial courts for hearings consistent with the statute. It affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals in the other appeal.

State v. Sewell


Case No. 08CA3042


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, ROSS COUNTY


2009-Ohio-594; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 509



February 5, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal allowed by State v. Sewell, 121 Ohio St. 3d 1500, 2009 Ohio 2511, 907 N.E.2d 324, 2009 Ohio LEXIS 1561 (2009)
Reversed by, in part, Remanded by In re Sexual Offender Reclassification Cases, 126 Ohio St. 3d 322, 2010 Ohio 3753, 933 N.E.2d 801, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 1931 (2010)


DISPOSITION:  [**1] JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of a judgment from the Ross County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which overruled his multiple challenges to his reclassification under R.C. 2950.031(E) as a Tier III sex offender pursuant to R.C. Chapter 2950, as amended by S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio 2008). The inmate had been previously convicted of rape and he was previously classified as a sexually oriented offender.


OVERVIEW: The inmate received notification of his new classification and registration duties, and he contested them under § 2950.031(E). The trial court overruled the inmate's constitutional challenges. On appeal, the court agreed that the inmate's challenges to the constitutionality of Chapter 2950 lacked merit. It noted that as Chapter 2950 was civil rather than punitive in nature, there was no violation of the retroactivity prohibition under Ohio Const. art. II, § 28. The civil nature of Chapter 2950 also defeated the inmate's claim that reclassification constituted multiple punishments in violation of the Double Jeopardy Clauses of U.S. Const. amend. V and Ohio Const. art. I, § 10. As the inmate had no reasonable settled expectation of his registration obligations from the prior classification, there was no due process violation. There was no violation of the separation of powers doctrine because S.B. 10 did not impose on the power of the judiciary. There was no violation of the contract clause of the Ohio Constitution, as S.B. 10 did not impair any of the inmate's vested rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

State ex rel. A.J. Rose Mfg. Co. v. Schwarz


No. 11AP-379


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2012-Ohio-4367; 2012 Ohio App. LEXIS 3836



September 25, 2012, Rendered


DISPOSITION:  [**1] Objections overruled; writ of mandamus granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator employer commenced an original action in mandamus for an order compelling respondent, the Industrial Commission of Ohio, to vacate its order that required the employer to pay respondent claimant the remaining balance of prior R.C. 4123.57(B) awards for loss of use of both arms and legs in one lump-sum payment pursuant to Ohio Admin. Code 4123-3-15(C)(3)(b). A magistrate recommended that the request was to be granted.


OVERVIEW: Effective October 12, 2010, Ohio Admin. Code 4123-3-15 (2010) was amended to require the payment of final R.C. 4123.57(B) awards in one payment for an entire award or the balance remaining on the award. The employer contended that Ohio Admin. Code 4123-3-15(C)(3) could not be applied retroactively because the provision failed to clearly indicate a retroactive application. The claimant, who was rendered a quadriplegic in an industrial injury, contended that Ohio Admin. Code 4123-3-15(C) expressly indicated that it had retroactive application to final awards made prior to its effective date. The reviewing court found that, in the absence of language clearly indicating that the administrative code provision was intended to apply retroactively to final awards made prior to its effective date, the rule could not be retroactively applied pursuant to Ohio Const. art. II, § 28. Furthermore, the provision affected a substantive right. Therefore, the employer was entitled to the writ of mandamus.


OUTCOME: The writ of mandamus was granted.

 

431 of 997 DOCUMENTS



Kaminski v. Metal & Wire Prods. Co.


No. 2008-0857


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


125 Ohio St. 3d 250; 2010-Ohio-1027; 927 N.E.2d 1066; 2010 Ohio LEXIS 727; 30 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 883; 159 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P60,963


February 18, 2009, Submitted 
March 23, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Columbiana County, No. 07-CO-15, 175 Ohio App.3d 227, 2008 Ohio 1521, 886 N.E.2d 262.
Kaminski v. Metal & Wire Prods. Co., 175 Ohio App. 3d 227, 2008 Ohio 1521, 886 N.E.2d 262, 2008 Ohio App. LEXIS 1369 (Ohio Ct. App., Columbiana County, 2008)



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee employee sued appellant employer for an employer intentional tort and asserted that R.C. 2745.01 was unconstitutional. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the employer, but the Seventh District Court of Appeals (Ohio) held R.C. 2745.01 unconstitutional in its entirety and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings. The employer sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held, in reliance on a prior decision regarding a prior version of R.C. 2745.01, that the statute violated Ohio Const. art. II, §§ 34 and 35. The supreme court held the prior decision's interpretation of Ohio Const. art. II, §§ 34 and 35 was flawed because it held those provisions limited the legislature's authority to enact employer intentional-tort legislation when, in fact, those provisions were affirmative grants of legislative authority in the areas of employment legislation and, specifically, workers' compensation, so the statute could not violate them, and Ohio Const. art. II, § 35 did not forbid legislation affecting employees' tort recovery without affecting employees' receipt of workers' compensation. The trial court properly granted summary judgment in favor of the employer because, construing all materials in a light most favorable to the employee, nothing showed she could prove the employer committed a tortious act with the intent to injure her or that the employer acted with deliberate intent to cause her to suffer an injury, for purposes of R.C. 2745.01(A) and (B), so, under R.C. 2745.01, there was no genuine issue as to any material fact.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the trial court's grant of summary judgment was reinstated.


 

432 of 997 DOCUMENTS


O'Brien v. Ohio Lottery Comm'n


CASE NOS. 2004-L-017 and 2004-L-018


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, ELEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, LAKE COUNTY


2005-Ohio-1412; 2005 Ohio App. LEXIS 1381



March 25, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Administrative Appeals from the Lake County Court of Common Pleas, Case Nos. 03 CV 000820 and 03 CV 000821.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Ohio Lottery Commission sought review of the judgment entry of the Lake County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) which reversed the Commission's revocation of the licenses of appellee sales agents and held that former Ohio Admin. Code § 3770-2-03(B)(1) was unconstitutionally vague on its face and as applied to the agents.


OVERVIEW: One of the agents extended credit to a friend to cover some wagers, including $ 490,000 over three days. The agent claimed that she was able to cover the cost of the bets with her own funds if the friend failed to pay for the loan. The agent telephoned another agent who placed a subsequent $ 40,000 wager for the friend on credit. The friend won some money, although not enough to cover the amount owed. Both of the friend's personal checks were dishonored. The agents had to make deposits of their own money into their respective lottery bank accounts to ensure that there were sufficient funds. The hearing examiner found that the agents violated former Ohio Admin. Code § 3770-2-03(B)(1) by accepting the wagers solely on the word of a customer. The appellate court held that it failed to see how the director's exclusive discretion to revoke a sales agent license would have aided an individual to conform his conduct to the regulation. Essentially, "financial responsibility" encompassed whatever conduct the director decided it should encompass. Thus, it violated due process of the laws.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

City of Toledo v. Tellings


Court of Appeals No. L-04-1224


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, LUCAS COUNTY


2006-Ohio-975; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 884



March 3, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay granted by Toledo v. Tellings, 109 Ohio St. 3d 1455, 2006 Ohio 2226, 847 N.E.2d 5, 2006 Ohio LEXIS 1387 (2006)
Discretionary appeal allowed by Toledo v. Tellings, 110 Ohio St. 3d 1437, 2006 Ohio 3862, 852 N.E.2d 186, 2006 Ohio LEXIS 2222 (2006)
Motion granted by Toledo v. Tellings, 112 Ohio St. 3d 1438, 2007 Ohio 152, 860 N.E.2d 763, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 73 (2007)
Motion granted by Toledo v. Tellings, 113 Ohio St. 3d 1411, 2007 Ohio 1036, 862 N.E.2d 841, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 533 (2007)
Motion granted by Toledo v. Tellings, 113 Ohio St. 3d 1423, 2007 Ohio 1313, 863 N.E.2d 642, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 665 (2007)
Motion granted by Toledo v. Tellings, 113 Ohio St. 3d 1445, 2007 Ohio 1430, 863 N.E.2d 660, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 786 (2007)
Reversed by City of Toledo v. Tellings, 2007 Ohio 3724, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 1850 (Ohio, Aug. 1, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Trial Court No. CRB-02-15267.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Toledo Municipal Court (Ohio), which convicted him of violations of Toledo, Ohio, Code § 505.14(a) and Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 955.22. The trial court found that the state and local "vicious dog" laws were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned three pit bull type dogs, which were family pets who had no history of aggressive or unlawful behavior. Upon a report of the dogs' presence being filed with the county dog warden, one dog was given away, one was confiscated and destroyed, and one was kept by defendant. Defendant was charged by the city with local and state statutory violations. The trial court denied defendant's dismissal motion, finding that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. §§ 955.22 and 955.11(A)(4)(a)(iii), and Toledo, Ohio, Code § 505.14(a) were constitutional, although it noted that pit bulls were not, as a breed, more dangerous than other breeds. On appeal, the court found that the statutes were unconstitutionally violative of procedural due process, as defendant could not rebut the presumption that his dogs were "vicious" before being charged. Further, the statutes were void for vagueness under that constitutional principle due to the subjective identification procedures. The statutes also violated equal protection and substantive due process where there was no rational basis to single out the pit bull as inherently dangerous, based on the current evidence regarding the breed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and vacated defendant's convictions.

City of Lakewood v. Calanni


No. 82119


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


154 Ohio App. 3d 703; 2003-Ohio-5246; 798 N.E.2d 701; 2003 Ohio App. LEXIS 4742



October 2, 2003, Date of Announcement of Decision


PRIOR HISTORY: CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Criminal appeal from Lakewood Municipal Court. Case No. 02B902.
City of Lake Wood v. Calanni, 2002 Ohio 7485, 2002 Ohio Misc. LEXIS 68 (Ohio Mun. Ct., Oct. 31, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was served with a complaint for having an immobilized and/or unlicensed vehicle on his property for more than three consecutive days, in violation of the Lakewood, Ohio, Codified Ordinance § 1143.09. After the Lakewood Municipal Court (Ohio) denied defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint, the owner pled no contest and was found guilty. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that that Lakewood, Ohio, Codified Ordinance § 1143.04(i)(9) exempted his automobile repair business as a "motor vehicle sales lot" and permitted immobilized or unlicensed vehicles to remain on his property for more than three consecutive days. The appellate court held that defendant owned a repair shop for automobiles that was not classified as a "motor vehicle sales lot." Therefore, he was not exempt from prosecution for violating Lakewood, Ohio, Codified Ordinance § 1143.09. Further, the Building Department's request for summons, incorporated into the face of the complaint, spelled out all of the essential facts of the offense and was valid within the meaning of Ohio R. Crim. P. 3. Finally, the complaint was not unconstitutionally vague for failing to state which condition existed that constituted the violation. It was clear that defendant was being cited for having a vehicle with expired license plates. Also, the nature and violation of the ordinance would have remained the same regardless of whether the vehicle was immobilized and had expired license plates.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

In re SW


No. 05AP-1368 (REGULAR CALENDAR)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2006-Ohio-2958; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 2829



June 13, 2006, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Z. W. v. Franklin County Children Servs., 127 S. Ct. 1152, 166 L. Ed. 2d 999, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 1222 (U.S., 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL from the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas, Division of Domestic Relations, Juvenile Branch. (C.P.C. No. 02JU-2171).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Franklin County Court of Common Pleas, Juvenile Division (Ohio), terminated respondent mother's parental rights to her child and placed him in the permanent custody of petitioner agency, pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.413, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.353(A)(4), and Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.414(B)(1)(d). The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother argued that the trial court erred in not declaring Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.414(B)(1)(d) unconstitutional since it created an irrebuttable presumption of parental unfitness and included that presumption in determining the best interests of the child. The appellate court held that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.414(B)(1)(d) was constitutional on its face. Further, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.413 and Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.414 did not violate the mother's equal protection rights because they did not distinguish between groups of parents or treat similarly situated parents differently. The mother's unsupported assertions of unconstitutionality were insufficient to satisfy her burden. Additionally, because Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2151.414(B)(1)(d) applied, in that the child had been in the agency's custody for 12 of 22 consecutive months, the trial court was not required to find parental unfitness before it terminated her parental rights. Finally, the trial court had sufficient evidence to find by clear and convincing evidence that the child's best interests were served by placing him in the permanent custody of the agency to facilitate his adoption into a permanent home.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

O'Brien v. Ohio Lottery Comm'n


CASE NOS. 2004-L-095 and 2004-L-096


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, ELEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, LAKE COUNTY


2005-Ohio-1564; 2005 Ohio App. LEXIS 1483

OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Blvd. v. Ohio Dep't of Heal Tii

438 of 997 DOCUMENTS


Zeigler v. Zumbar


No. 2010-1570


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


129 Ohio St. 3d 240; 2011-Ohio-2939; 951 N.E.2d 405; 2011 Ohio LEXIS 1646


March 23, 2011, Submitted 
June 23, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State ex rel. Zeigler v. Zumbar, 129 Ohio St. 3d 1454, 2011 Ohio 4217, 951 N.E.2d 1049, 2011 Ohio LEXIS 2087 (Ohio, Aug. 24, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:
In Quo Warranto.
State ex rel. Zeigler v. Allbritain, 128 Ohio St. 3d 1418, 2011 Ohio 976, 942 N.E.2d 1106, 2011 Ohio LEXIS 605 (2011)



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the chief deputy treasurer stole $2,964,560 from the county treasury, the board of commissioners voted to remove relator former county treasurer for failing to pay over money pursuant to R.C. 321.38. An election was held and respondent current county treasurer was elected. The former treasurer filed an action for a writ of quo warranto, seeking to oust the current county treasurer from office and to be reinstated to that office.


OVERVIEW: The current treasurer asserted that the former treasurer's quo warranto claim was both moot and barred by laches. The state supreme court held that the fact that there had been three successors since the former treasurer's removal did not bar his quo warranto claim. The former treasurer's term of office had not expired, he lacked an adequate remedy in the ordinary course of law, and the proceedings were not quasi-judicial. Further, he satisfied the burden to establish that R.C. 321.38 was unconstitutional on its face. R.C. 321.38 authorized the removal of elected county treasurers from office by county commissioners without the due process protections of a complaint and hearing that were required by Ohio Const. art. II, § 38. R.C. 321.38 was thus, irreconcilable with the constitutional provision. And, although the current treasurer was elected to the office, that midterm election occurred only because of the former treasurer's improper removal. Because R.C. 321.38 was unconstitutional, the removal of the former treasurer violated Ohio Const. art. II, § 38 and he was entitled to serve the remainder of his elected term, set to expire in September 2013.


OUTCOME: The writ of quo warranto to oust from office the current county treasurer and reinstate the former treasurer to office was granted.

 

439 of 997 DOCUMENTS


In re E.D.


C.A. No. 25594


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, NINTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, SUMMIT COUNTY


194 Ohio App. 3d 534; 2011-Ohio-4067; 957 N.E.2d 80; 2011 Ohio App. LEXIS 3415



August 17, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL FROM JUDGMENT ENTERED IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS COUNTY OF SUMMIT, OHIO. CASE No. DL 10 05 1275, DL 10 05 1277.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile was arrested for loitering for the purpose of engaging in drug-related activity, in violation of Akron, Ohio, Codified Ordinance § 138.26. He filed a motion to dismiss asserting that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The Summit County Court of Common Pleas, Juvenile Division (Ohio), entered an order dismissing the charges and finding the ordinance unconstitutionally void for vagueness and overbroad. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the trial court erred in finding Akron, Ohio, Codified Ordinance § 138.26 unconstitutional. The appellate court held that the revised ordinance still allowed police officers to subjectively determine whether or not a citizen exhibited certain "characteristics" and thus were given unfettered discretion to determine whether a person's conduct "manifested" drug activity, which invited arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement and rendered the ordinance unconstitutionally void for vagueness. The "specific intent" addition did not clearly specify what conduct an individual has the specific intent to engage in and failed to provide guidance as to how a citizen was to determine whether the area was known to the officer as a "high drug activity geographic area." Further, allowing officers, judges, and juries to consider the enumerated circumstances in revised Akron, Ohio, Codified Ordinance § 138.26(B) swept within the prohibitions of the ordinance many things that may not be constitutionally punished under the First and Fourteenth Amendments. Accordingly, the revised ordinance was impermissibly overbroad.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Love


CASE NO. 06 MA 130


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, MAHONING COUNTY


2007-Ohio-7210; 2007 Ohio App. LEXIS 6307



December 21, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by State v. Love, 118 Ohio St. 3d 1409, 2008 Ohio 2340, 886 N.E.2d 872, 2008 Ohio LEXIS 1377 (May 21, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Criminal Appeal from Common Pleas Court, Case No. 98CR919.
In re Ohio Crim. Sentencing Statutes Cases, 109 Ohio St. 3d 313, 2006 Ohio 2109, 847 N.E.2d 1174, 2006 Ohio LEXIS 1161 (May 3, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Mahoning County Common Pleas Court (Ohio), which sentenced defendant to a maximum sentence for a community control violation. The sentence was imposed during a Foster remand. The five-year sentence was to run consecutive to the sentence imposed on the aggravated murder conviction that led to the community control revocation.


OVERVIEW: The trial court's sentencing entry stated that it considered the purposes and principles of sentencing under R.C. 2929.11 and balanced the seriousness and recidivism factors under R.C. 2929.12. Defendant's sole assignment of error was whether the trial court erred in imposing a maximum sentence. The appellate court affirmed. The trial court had full discretion to sentence within the statutory range, and a mention of factors that were required pre-Foster would not be erroneous because the trial court could consider any factors it wanted to in sentencing. In defendant's case, the transcript did not reflect any mention of any previously required factors. While not properly preserved, defendant's ex post facto and due process claims also failed. The Foster remedy was not unconstitutionally retroactive. The sentencing statutes created, at most, a presumption of minimum, concurrent sentences. The presumption was not guaranteed, and therefore, there was no vested right to a minimum sentence. The state supreme court mandated the retroactive application of Foster to cases pending on direct review. The appellate court was bound by that mandate.


OUTCOME: The appellate court overruled defendant's assignment of error and affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Nixon


C. A. No. 22667


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, NINTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, SUMMIT COUNTY


165 Ohio App. 3d 178; 2006-Ohio-72; 845 N.E.2d 544; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 55



January 11, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM JUDGMENT ENTERED IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS, COUNTY OF SUMMIT, OHIO. CASE No. CR 05 01 0359.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Summit County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which denied his motion to dismiss. Defendant was convicted of domestic violence and resisting arrest, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. §§ 2919.25(A) and 2921.33(A), respectively. Two other counts against defendant were dismissed.


OVERVIEW: The charges against defendant arose from an incident involving his girlfriend, with whom defendant resided. Defendant entered not guilty pleas and sought dismissal of the charges. In support of dismissal, defendant claimed that the charged offenses were unconstitutional as applied to him pursuant to the Marriage Protection Amendment (MPA), Ohio Const. art. XV, § 11. Although it was not specified, the motion to dismiss was apparently based on the domestic violence count. The trial court denied dismissal and defendant entered guilty pleas to two charges. The trial court held that the domestic violence statute was constitutional, and it imposed sentence on defendant. On appeal, the court found that the victim was within the coverage of a "family or household member" under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2919.25(A)-(C). The court agreed that the statute was constitutional, in that the intent and purpose of the domestic violence statute was not altered by the language of the MPA. Each legislative enactment had a different purpose and protected a separate right. Accordingly, defendant did not meet his stringent burden of proving that the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

443 of 997 DOCUMENTS


State v. Stallings


C.A. No. 20987


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, NINTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, SUMMIT COUNTY


150 Ohio App. 3d 5; 2002-Ohio-5942; 778 N.E.2d 1110; 2002 Ohio App. LEXIS 5762



October 30, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM JUDGMENT ENTERED IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS COUNTY OF SUMMIT, OHIO. CASE No. CR 00 07 1483.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled no contest to possession of cocaine, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2925.11(A), and criminal gang activity, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2923.42(A). The Summit County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) accepted the plea and sentenced him accordingly. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2923.42(A) violated the United States and Ohio Constitutions because it was vague, criminalized membership in an organization, and inflicted cruel and unusual punishment. The appellate court held that defendant failed to satisfy his burden of establishing unconstitutionality. The common and ordinary meaning of "actively participates in a criminal gang" was involvement with a criminal gang that was more than nominal or passive. Further, for defendant to be criminally liable under § 2923.42(A), he also had to be criminally liable as an aider or abettor to a crime committed by a fellow gang member or members. Accordingly, "purposely promoted, furthered, or assisted any criminal conduct" was not void for vagueness and was well-defined under Ohio law. Also, § 2923.42(A) did not unconstitutionally criminalize membership in an organization because the statute did not impermissibly establish guilt by association alone and the right to association, as guaranteed by the United States and Ohio Constitutions, did not provide protection to those organizations that commit a felony.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

In re M.D.


CASE NO. CA2006-09-223


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TWELFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, BUTLER COUNTY


2007-Ohio-4646; 2007 Ohio App. LEXIS 4181



September 10, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM BUTLER COUNTY COURT OF COMMON PLEAS, JUVENILE DIVISION. Case No. JN2004-0072.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the child was adjudicated abused and neglected, by her natural father, appellee paternal grandparents were granted temporary custody. Appellee children's services agency filed a motion for legal custody on behalf of the grandparents. The Butler County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), based on a magistrate's decision, granted the grandparents legal custody and granted appellant mother visitation. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother challenged the constitutionality of R.C. 2151.353(A)(3). The appellate court held that the mother failed to demonstrate that R.C. 2151.353(A)(3) was unconstitutional as she failed to show that it violated due process requirements. The trial court properly applied the best interest of the child standard, set forth in R.C. 3109.04(F), and found that it was in the child's best interest to grant the grandparents legal custody of the child and to allow the mother visitation. The trial court was not required to find the mother unsuitable before making that disposition as the previous adjudication of the child as abused and dependent implicitly involved a finding of the mother's unsuitability. Further, granting legal custody of the child to the grandparents was in the child's best interest. The trial court thoroughly considered both the beneficial and detrimental aspects of placing the child with either party in making its best interest determination. It found that it was in her best interest to be placed in a stable environment with parental figures who could provide and model appropriate behavior, and understand her psychological needs resulting from her abuse by her father.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Cameron v. State


Case No. 2008CA0180


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, RICHLAND COUNTY


2009-Ohio-3342; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 2876



June 30, 2009, Date of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 2008CV553D.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Ohio sought review of a decision from the Richland County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which granted judgment to appellee inmate in his action under R.C. 2950.031(E) and 2950.032(E), contesting his sex offender reclassification. The trial court held that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio 2008), was unconstitutional facially and as applied to the inmate.


OVERVIEW: The inmate received notification that his sex offender status was being reclassified. He filed his petition to contest the level of his classification and the application of the Adam Walsh Act pursuant to §§ 2950.031(E) and 2950.032(E). It was noted that R.C. 2950.01 et seq., as amended by S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio 2008), was not in effect when the inmate committed the sexual offenses, although it was in effect on the date that he was reclassified. The trial court held that S.B. 10 was unconstitutional facially and as applied to the inmate. The State appealed, and the matter was stayed pending resolution of a pending matter that raised the same issues. In the pending matter, the court found that S.B. 10 was constitutional and withstood challenges based on ex post facto laws, the right to contract, the separation of powers doctrine, double jeopardy, and substantive and procedural due process. Accordingly, the court determined that the inmate's constitutional challenges lacked merit. The court noted that the Adam Walsh Act withstood challenge in virtually every appellate district court in Ohio.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

449 of 997 DOCUMENTS


Land Dev. Mgmt., LLC v. City of Lancaster


Case No. 11-CA-47


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FAIRFIELD COUNTY


2012-Ohio-3136; 2012 Ohio App. LEXIS 2768



July 9, 2012, Date of Judgment


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 10CV1367.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After appellant land development management company's request for a use variance was denied, it filed a declaratory judgment action against appellee city challenging the constitutionality of a zoning classification as applied to its property. The Fairfield County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) granted summary judgment for the city. The company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The company argued that the trial court erred in granting summary judgment to the city. The appellate court held that the trial court did not err in granting summary judgment to the city because the zoning as it pertained to the company's property was not unconstitutional. The company's property was not singled out to a more restrictive use, but was given a more restrictive use given the entire area. When viewed in light of the general purposes clauses of the two zoning classifications, the distinction was reasonable. Those included in a CG (commercial general district) that are not in a CN (commercial neighborhood district) create high volumes of customer traffic and have a higher impact on adjacent uses. Therefore, the delineation between the two classifications was reasonable and not arbitrary and did have a relation to the public safety and health. Further, there was no violation of equal protection because the city set forth a legitimate governmental interest in the two, albeit similar, zoning classifications. There was also no convincing evidence that the company's property and the property it used for comparison were similarly situated properties.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

450 of 997 DOCUMENTS


Stalker v. Indus. Comm'n


No. 03AP-788


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2004-Ohio-1144; 2004 Ohio App. LEXIS 968



March 11, 2004, Rendered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] (C.P.C. No. 99CVH-10-8750). APPEAL from the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Franklin County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) granted summary judgment to defendants, the Industrial Commission of Ohio and the Administrator of the Ohio Bureau of Workers' Compensation, on a complaint by plaintiff, a worker, alleging that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4123.56 violated the worker's right to equal protection of law in specifying a cap on benefits based on the state-wide average weekly wage. The worker appealed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The worker alleged that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4123.56 was unconstitutional insofar as it placed a cap on disability benefits. Applying the rational basis test, the trial court determined that defendants identified a legitimate purpose for establishing a wage cap for the payment of compensation. The appellate court held that the workers' compensation legislation could survive constitutional scrutiny under the federal and Ohio equal protection clauses if the statute was rationally related to the accomplishment of some state objective at least as important as the purpose contained in the Constitution. A statute had to be upheld against an equal protection challenge if there was any reasonably conceivable state of facts that could provide a rational basis for the classification. The appellate court found that in Ohio, workers' compensation was designed to provide sustenance benefits only. By including a wage cap in Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4123.56, the appellate court held that the legislature promoted actuarial soundness and balance, while at the same time directing the sustenance-level benefits to those who most needed it. Thus, there was a rational basis for the wage cap.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.
