Wymsylo v. Bartec, Inc.


No. 2011-0019


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


132 Ohio St. 3d 167; 2012-Ohio-2187; 970 N.E.2d 898; 2012 Ohio LEXIS 1279; 162 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P61,256


October 19, 2011, Submitted 
May 23, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Wymsylo v. Bartec, Inc., 132 Ohio St. 3d 1485, 2012 Ohio 3334, 971 N.E.2d 962, 2012 Ohio LEXIS 1932 (Ohio, July 25, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 10AP-173,
Jackson v. Bartec, Inc., 2010 Ohio 5558, 2010 Ohio App. LEXIS 4692 (Ohio Ct. App., Franklin County, Nov. 16, 2010).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case involving constitutional challenges to the Ohio Smoke Free Workplace Act, the Ohio Court of Appeals, Tenth District, Franklin County, held that the Act was constitutional. The Supreme Court accepted the appeal from appellants.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court held that the Smoke Free Workplace Act is a valid exercise of the state's police power by Ohio's voters and does not constitute a taking of property. Appellants failed to establish that they would be subject to an unlawful policy of strict liability. Substantial evidence existed that appellants at least implicitly permitted smoking. A city health department investigator witnessed two people smoking at a bar and observed cigarette butts in plastic cups filled halfway with water. Another investigator found multiple bar patrons who were smoking and who were using partially filled plastic cups as ashtrays. Although one of the appellants was present at the time, the investigator did not witness him address any of the smoking patrons. A third investigator witnessed at least eight patrons smoking and using small plastic cups as ashtrays. Although appellants were required under R.C. 3794.06(B) to remove all ashtrays and other receptacles used for disposing of smoking materials, appellants were cited nine times for allowing ashtrays to be present. In eight of the violations, the investigator determined that the violation was intentional and doubled the fine.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

State v. Black


Court of Appeals No. H-01-033


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, HURON COUNTY


2002-Ohio-731; 2002 Ohio App. LEXIS 749



February 22, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Trial Court No. CRI-00-0934.



DISPOSITION: Judgment Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was arrested pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2935.26. He filed a motion to suppress the evidence, which was denied. The Huron County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) convicted defendant for drug possession, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2925.11(A). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The police officers saw defendant staggering on the sidewalk and cited him for disorderly intoxication. He was then arrested because he appeared unable to provide for his on safety and was at risk of harm if left to walk the streets. The appellate court held that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2935.26(A)(1), which permitted arrest of a person for the commission of a minor misdemeanor if the offender was unable to provide for his own safety, was not unconstitutional. Defendant failed to establish that the statute, which ensured citizens' safety, limited any constitutionally protected fundamental rights, either facially or in its application. Further, according to the officer, defendant was staggering, almost fell into the street, was unsteady when trying to stand in one place, and had other physical indicators that he was intoxicated. Accordingly, sufficient evidence was presented from which the trial court could find that defendant could not provide for his own safety and the arrest was lawful. Therefore, the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppress the evidence found during the inventory search.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State ex rel. Pilarczyk v. City of Riverside


C.A. CASE NO. 20706


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, MONTGOMERY COUNTY


2005-Ohio-3755; 2005 Ohio App. LEXIS 3474



July 22, 2005, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by Pilarczyk v. Riverside, 2005 Ohio 6480, 2005 Ohio LEXIS 2873 (Ohio, Dec. 14, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] (Civil Appeal from Common Pleas Court). T.C. NO. 03 CV 3487.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The zoning administrator for appellee city determined that appellant high school was required to install sidewalks and curbs along a street as a condition of a permit allowing the high school to construct an addition to its existing school building. The Montgomery County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) affirmed a decision of the Board of Zoning Appeals (BZA), which had upheld the zoning administrator's determination. The high school appealed.


OVERVIEW: The high school argued that the trial court erred in construing Riverside, Ohio, City Ordinance § 1341.01, which required a building permit applicant to install sidewalks when the proposed addition totaled 25 percent or more of the existing "square footage" of the principal existing building. The trial court found that the term "square footage" was ambiguous and affirmed the BZA's interpretation of "square footage" to mean the building's footprint. The appellate court found no error in the trial court's conclusion that "square footage" was ambiguous as used in § 1341.01. Rational arguments could have been made that the term could mean either the usable square footage or the footprint square footage. There was no evidence to support the high school's assertion that the trial court failed to consider the complete record, and consideration of other ordinances to construe § 1341.01 was proper. The BZA and the trial court reasonably concluded that "footprint square footage," rather than "gross floor area," was the intended meaning. The high school failed to present any evidence that it was treated differently from other properties in the city that constructed building additions.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Ritchey

City of Toledo v. Tellings


No. 2006-0690


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


114 Ohio St. 3d 278; 2007-Ohio-3724; 871 N.E.2d 1152; 2007 Ohio LEXIS 1850


April 4, 2007, Submitted 
August 1, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Toledo v. Tellings, 115 Ohio St. 3d 1414, 2007 Ohio 4884, 873 N.E.2d 1317, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 2267 (2007)
Petition for certiorari filed at, 12/20/2007
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Tellings v. City of Toledo, 552 U.S. 1225, 128 S. Ct. 1302, 170 L. Ed. 2d 140, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 2006 (U.S., Feb. 19, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Lucas County, No. L-04-1224, 2006 Ohio 975.
City of Toledo v. Tellings, 2006 Ohio 975, 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 884 (Ohio Ct. App., Lucas County, Mar. 3, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee was charged in the trial court with violating Toledo, Ohio, Mun. Code 505.14(a) and R.C. 955.22. The trial court denied his motion to dismiss, which alleged the provisions were unconstitutional, but the Court of Appeals for Lucas County (Ohio) reversed and found Toledo, Ohio, Mun. Code 505.14, R.C. 955.11 and R.C. 955.22 unconstitutional. Appellant, the City of Toledo, sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held the statutes and ordinance violated procedural due process, substantive due process, and equal protection. The supreme court held the provisions did not violate substantive due process or equal protection because the State of Ohio and the City had a legitimate interest in protecting citizens from unsafe conditions caused by pit bulls, as substantial evidence showed they caused disproportionate danger to people, and the laws were rationally related to those interests when the dogs' owners had to obtain liability insurance and were barred from surgically silencing them, and, under the ordinance, the number of pit bulls per person or entity was limited. The provisions did not violate procedural due process because pit bulls were classified generally as vicious dogs, so there was no unilateral, case-by-case decision-making, so all pit bull owners knew that failure to abide by the laws related to pit bulls was a crime. The provisions were not void for vagueness, on the theory that one could not tell if one owned a pit bull, because the dogs' traits and commonly available information about dog breeds sufficiently informed owners whether they owned a pit bull.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed.

McGhee v. State


Case No. 2008-CA-255


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, RICHLAND COUNTY


2009-Ohio-4353; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 3691



August 25, 2009, Date of Judgment Entry


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Civil appeal from the Richland County Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 08-CV-146D.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee sex offender received a Notice of New Classification and Registration Duties based on Ohio's Adam Walsh Act, R.C. 2950.01, et seq., reclassifying him as a Tier III sex offender, which he challenged. The Richland County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) found S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional both facially and as applied as it violated the prohibitions against both retroactive and ex post facto laws. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the trial court erred when it found that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional. The appellate court held that the trial court erred by finding that S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) was unconstitutional. S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) which amended R.C. 2950.01, et seq., was constitutional and thus, the Adam Walsh Act did not violate prohibitions against retroactive or ex post facto laws. Also, there was no violation of the right to contract pursuant to Ohio Const. art. II, § 28.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the cause was remanded for further proceedings.

Cahill v. Testa

2016-Ohio-7648; 2016 Ohio App. LEXIS 4522

OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court did not err by dismissing the couple's Equal Protection claim and class action suit for failure to state a claim because, at the relevant times. Ecological Sys. v. City of Dayton


C.A. CASE NO. 18950, 18951, 18966


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, MONTGOMERY COUNTY


2002-Ohio-388; 2002 Ohio App. LEXIS 354



February 1, 2002, Rendered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] T.C. CASE NO. 00-1214.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment was affirmed, in part, reversed, in part, and cause was remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff water treatment plant appealed the judgment of the Montgomery County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) finding that the plant's wastewater was hauled waste and that the city municipal ordinance, prohibiting waste from outside the city, did not violate the Ohio Constitution. The city cross-appealed the finding that the city municipal ordinance violated the United States Constitution's Commerce Clause.


OVERVIEW: The city maintained a publicly owned treatment works (POTW). The plant intended to haul contaminated wastewater from industrial users within Ohio and surrounding states to the city's POTW. Then, the plant was to use the city's POTW facilities to discharge the wastewater after it was treated. The plant was required to obtain a permit to discharge into the city's POTW. The applications were denied based on Dayton, Ohio, Mun. Ordinance § 52.06(B)(3), (4). The trial court erred in concluding that the wastewater the plant sought to discharge into the city's POTW was excluded by § 52.06(B)(3), (4). The wastewater did not constitute hauled waste under § 52.06(B)(3), (4). The trial court did not err in finding that § 52.06(B)(3), (4), which prohibited discharge from outside the city's area, did not violate the Ohio Constitution. Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 6111 provided the city with an express grant of discretion when enacting local ordinances. Section 52.06(B)(3), (4) violated the dormant Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution. The ordinance patently discriminated against interstate commerce, and there was no health or safety reason necessitating the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded for further proceedings.

526 of 997 DOCUMENTS


State v. Worst


CASE NO. CA2004-10-270


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TWELFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, BUTLER COUNTY


2005-Ohio-6550; 2005 Ohio App. LEXIS 5900



December 12, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CRIMINAL APPEAL FROM BUTLER COUNTY COURT OF COMMON PLEAS. Case No. CR2004-01-0125.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Butler County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) of importuning and attempted unlawful sexual conduct with a minor, under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. §§ 2907.07(D)(2), 2923.02(A) and 2907.04(A), and the trial court classified him as a "sexually oriented offender." He appealed his classification and convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant proposed, on the internet, having sex with an officer he thought was a 14-year-old girl. The appellate court held registration requirements of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. §§ 2950.04 through 2950.06 were not unconstitutional as applied to sexually-oriented offenders because they rationally exercised the State's police powers. As defendant's intended victim was under 18, his crime was not presumptively registration-exempt, under Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2950.01(P)(1). Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2907.07(D)(2), prohibiting importuning, was not facially unconstitutional, as it was not void for vagueness. It clearly prohibited adults from using computers to solicit minors of certain ages, or law enforcement personnel posing as such minors, for sex. It barred conduct, not "virtual" child pornography, and was narrowly tailored to serve the government's compelling interest. It was not unjust to require the State only to prove defendant thought the person he communicated with was a minor. The First Amendment did not protect such conduct. As he stipulated that he thought the person he communicated with was 14, his conviction was not against the manifest weight of the evidence.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and classification were affirmed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Franklin County Court of Common Pleas, Juvenile Division, Juvenile Branch(Ohio), terminated the parental rights of appellants, the mother of all the children and the father to one of the children, to the children and placed the children in the permanent custody of appellee agency, pursuant to R.C. § 2151.414. OVERVIEW: Appellants argued that the trial court erred in terminating the parental rights of the parents and in granting permanent custody to the agency. It was clear from the record that since the agency became involved with the family, the parents had failed to establish a stable and safe home and income sufficient to meet the children's needs and failed to remedy the situation that caused the removal of the children.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Barnes


NO. 77078


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


2001-Ohio-4138; 2001 Ohio App. LEXIS 85



January 11, 2001, Date of Announcement of Decision


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Criminal appeal from Common Pleas Court, Case No. CR-367949.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the sentence imposed upon him by the Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) subsequent to his guilty plea to involuntary manslaughter in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.04, arguing his sentence violated his federal constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pleaded guilty to involuntary manslaughter in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.04. The trial court sentenced him to a term of imprisonment of 5 to 25 years. Appellant challenged his sentence, arguing because he was sentenced under pre-Am. Sub. S.B. 2 (Ohio) law, he was denied due process and equal protection of law as guaranteed by the United States Constitution on account of his potential to receive an increased length of sentence at the whim of the Ohio Parole Authority. He also argued under the present parole scheme it was not unrealistic to anticipate that he would serve every day of his 25-year maximum term, yet another defendant who committed the exact offense after the effective date of Am. Sub. S.B. 2 (Ohio) would only serve a maximum of 10 years. The court affirmed, holding Am. Sub. S.B. 2 (Ohio) was inapplicable to appellant's crime where it was committed prior to its effective date, and standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute required demonstration of concrete injury in fact, rather than an abstract or suspected injury.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed, because the sentencing guideline relied upon by appellant was inapplicable to his crime where it was committed prior to its effective date. Further, standing to challenge the constitutionality of a statute required demonstration of concrete injury in fact, rather than an abstract or suspected injury.

In re J.M.


No. 91800


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


2009-Ohio-2880; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 2432



June 18, 2009, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal allowed by, Reserved by In re J.M., 123 Ohio St. 3d 1507, 2009 Ohio 6210, 917 N.E.2d 811, 2009 Ohio LEXIS 3411 (2009)
Reversed by, in part, Remanded by In re Sexual Offender Reclassification Cases, 2010 Ohio 3753, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 1931 (Ohio, Aug. 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Civil Appeal from the Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas. Juvenile Division. Case No. DL 08121170.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas. Juvenile Division (Ohio), which denied his petition contesting the application to him of the new classification and registration requirements of Ohio's sex offender statutes.


OVERVIEW: As a juvenile, appellant was adjudicated a delinquent by virtue of committing the crime of rape. Two years after he was found to be a habitual sex offender, he was sent a notice of new classification and registration duties, notifying that, pursuant to the Ohio Legislature's passage of S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio), implementing the Adam Walsh Child Protection and Safety Act of 2006 (AWA), he would be newly classified as a Tier III sex offender. The court held that, pursuant to R.C. 2950.11(F)(2), the trial court should have relieved appellant of the notification requirements because he was exempt from community notification under the AWA since he was not subject to community notification under the former statute. The court rejected defendant's challenge to the constitutionality of S.B. 10, finding that it did not violate ex post facto protections, the retroactivity clause of the Ohio Constitution, and the Double Jeopardy Clause of the Ohio Constitution. S.B. 10 also did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers as the sex offender classification was a creation of the legislature, and therefore, the power to classify was properly expanded or limited by the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment insofar as it upheld the imposition of community notification; however, it affirmed the remainder of the trial court's judgment. The court remanded the matter for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

June 26, 2009, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Civil Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 2007 CV 0315.
Smith v. Inland Paperboard & Packaging, Inc., 2008 Ohio 6984, 2008 Ohio App. LEXIS 5805 (Ohio Ct. App., Portage County, Dec. 31, 2008)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured employee filed an industrial intentional tort suit against appellee employer. The Portage County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) granted the employer's motion for summary judgment. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee argued that the trial court erred by granting summary judgment for the employer. The appellate court held that the trial court did not err by granting the employer's motion for summary judgment. R.C. 2745.01 did not violate Ohio Const. art. II, §§ 34 or 35 and was, thus, constitutional on its face. The flexibility of constitutional decision-making and stare decisis directly applied. The employee was experienced in working around paperboard machines and was aware of the dangers presented. He knew, through both the training policies and his own experience, to not go into a machine while its moving parts were still under power.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State ex rel. Walgate v. Kasich


No. 12AP-548


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2013-Ohio-946; 989 N.E.2d 140; 2013 Ohio App. LEXIS 844



March 14, 2013, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal allowed by, Review pending at State ex rel. Walgate v. Kasich, 136 Ohio St. 3d 1449, 2013-Ohio-3210, 991 N.E.2d 256, 2013 Ohio LEXIS 1802 (2013)
Appeal dismissed by, in part State ex rel. Walgate v. Kasich, 140 Ohio St. 3d 1412, 2014-Ohio-3785, 2014 Ohio LEXIS 2122, 15 N.E.3d 881 (2014)
Reversed by, in part, Affirmed by, in part, Remanded by State ex rel. Walgate v. Kasich, 2016-Ohio-1176, 2016 Ohio LEXIS 874 (Ohio, Mar. 24, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
APPEAL from the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas. (C.P.C. No. 11CVH-10-13126).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought declaratory relief, injunctive relief, and a writ of mandamus, alleging, among other things, that Ohio's gambling laws unconstitutionally created a monopoly. Appellees, the Governor and the Tax Commissioner, the Casino Commission, and the Lottery Commission, argued appellants lacked standing. The Franklin County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) granted appellees' motion to dismiss. Appellants filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellants argued that the trial court erred in concluding that each appellant lacked standing. The appellate court held that the trial court properly dismissed the case for failure to state a claim under Civ.R. 12(B)(6) because none of appellants had private standing because they had not suffered or were not threatened with any direct and concrete injury in a manner or degree different from that suffered by the public in general. Although two appellants argued that, due to the gambling addiction of one of them, they and their family had suffered, the complaint did not allege that the laws in question had caused the injury or that the relief requested would redress such injury. Further, the complaint did not allege any special interest in a special fund, nor did it challenge the administration of a special fund. Rather, the complaint challenged the fact that some Ohio industries were being taxed differently than others, which was not sufficient to confer standing because it failed to allege damage distinct from the damages suffered by the general public and failed to allege a special interest in a special fund.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Miller


APPEAL NO. C-050821


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, HAMILTON COUNTY


2006-Ohio-2337; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 2205



May 12, 2006, Date of Judgment Entry on Appeal


NOTICE:

 [**1] THESE ARE NOT OFFICIAL HEADNOTES OR SYLLABI AND ARE NEITHER APPROVED IN ADVANCE NOR ENDORSED BY THE COURT. PLEASE REVIEW THE CASE IN FULL.


PRIOR HISTORY: Criminal Appeal From: Hamilton County Court of Common Pleas. TRIAL NO. B-0502600-C.



DISPOSITION: Sentence Vacated and Cause Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Hamilton County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which convicted him of two first-degree misdemeanor assaults, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.13(A). The trial court imposed consecutive maximum terms of incarceration.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered into a plea bargain, wherein he pled guilty to two misdemeanor charges. The trial court imposed maximum terms of incarceration which were to run consecutively, pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.22(C). On appeal, the court noted that the Foster holding applied to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.14(C), which required the same findings for sentence imposition when felony convictions were involved that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.22(C) required for purposes of misdemeanor sentencing. As the Ohio Supreme Court had declared Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.14(C) unconstitutional due to the judicial fact-finding involved, the court similarly held that Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2929.22(C) was unconstitutional. Accordingly, the sentence imposed on defendant was void and resentencing was mandated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction by the trial court, and it reversed the judgment as to the sentences imposed. The court vacated those sentences and remanded the matter for resentencing.

535 of 997 DOCUMENTS


Rumpke Sanitary Landfill, Inc. v. State


No. 2009-2004


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


128 Ohio St. 3d 41; 2010-Ohio-6037; 941 N.E.2d 1161; 2010 Ohio LEXIS 3062


September 15, 2010, Submitted 
December 16, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Rumpke Sanitary Landfill, Inc. v. State, 2010 Ohio 6140, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 3158 (Ohio, Dec. 16, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Hamilton County, Nos. C-081097 and C-081119, 184 Ohio App.3d 135, 2009 Ohio 4888, 919 N.E.2d 826.
Rumpke Sanitary Landfill, Inc. v. State, 184 Ohio App. 3d 135, 2009 Ohio 4888, 919 N.E.2d 826, 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 4147 (Ohio Ct. App., Hamilton County, 2009)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a town and its trustees, challenged a decision of the First District Court of Appeals for Hamilton County, Ohio which affirmed the trial court judgment denying the town's motion to intervene in an action for declaratory and injunctive relief filed by appellee landfill against the State of Ohio, denying the State's motion to dismiss for failure to join the town, and declaring amendments to R.C. 303.211 and 519.211 unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The issue presented on appeal was whether a township was a necessary party to a declaratory-judgment action challenging an enactment of the General Assembly as violating the one-subject rule of the Ohio Constitution. The court found that although the town may have had a practical interest in the outcome of the landfill's declaratory-judgment action, a township had no legal interest in the General Assembly's authority to enact laws and therefore was not a necessary party to a declaratory-judgment action challenging legislation as violating the one-subject rule. Although the town's interest in its pending litigation with the landfill may have been practically affected by the landfill's declaratory-judgment action against the State, the town had no legal interest in the authority of the General Assembly to enact amendments to R.C. 303.211 and 519.211 as part of an appropriations bill, in violation of the one-subject rule of the Ohio Constitution. Therefore, the town was not a necessary party to the landfill's declaratory-judgment action.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

 

536 of 997 DOCUMENTS


In re Protest of Brooks


CASE NO. 1-03-16, CASE NO. 9-03-19


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT, ALLEN COUNTY


155 Ohio App. 3d 370; 2003-Ohio-6348; 801 N.E.2d 503; 2003 Ohio App. LEXIS 5728



November 24, 2003, Date of Judgment Entry


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected April 5, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Civil Appeal from Common Pleas Court.
In re Protest of Brooks, 154 Ohio App. 3d 739, 2003 Ohio 5241, 798 N.E.2d 1118, 2003 Ohio App. LEXIS 4736 (Ohio Ct. App., Ashland County, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part in Allen County case; judgment affirmed in Marion County case.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, the Allen County (Ohio) Board of Elections and the Marion County (Ohio) Board of Elections, filed separate actions seeking a judgment determining if part-petitions submitted by appellant coalition were valid. The Allen County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) and the Marion County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) invalidated several part-petitions for failure to comply with Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3519.05, and the coalition appealed.


OVERVIEW: A coalition which supported legislation regulating the cost of prescription drugs circulated a petition asking voters to put the legislation to a vote. A person who opposed the initiative filed a protest with two county election boards, and both boards filed an action in the court of common pleas, seeking an order verifying several part-petitions. In separate decisions, both courts found that several part-petitions were invalid. The appellate court held that (1) the provision of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3519.05 which required people who circulated initiative petitions to attach a compensation statement to the petition did not violate the Ohio or United States Constitutions; (2) the trial courts had jurisdiction to evaluate the circulators' compensation statements when it reviewed the part-petitions; and (3) with one exception, the trial courts ruled correctly that the part-petitions were invalid because they did not contain an adequate statement of compensation, did not contain the permanent address of the person who compensated a person who circulated a part-petition, or because a person who circulated a part-petition allowed people who signed the petition to sign for others.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of one of the trial courts, and affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgment of the other trial court and remanded that case for further proceedings.

State v. Kutys


No. 01AP-1392


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2002-Ohio-4016; 2002 Ohio App. LEXIS 4204



August 8, 2002, Rendered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL from the Franklin County Municipal Court.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Franklin County Municipal Court (Ohio) that convicted defendant of one count of littering, a third degree misdemeanor, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 3767.32(A).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was employed as a delivery driver for a business. Defendant was fueling a truck at the company property's fueling station when he realized that the gasoline-powered truck was being filled instead with diesel fuel. Upon this realization, he stopped fueling, and began draining the diesel fuel from the truck into a two-gallon bucket. He then used a 15-gallon trash can until all the diesel was drained. He placed the trash can of diesel fuel near the tanks at the company's fueling station. At some point during this draining process, some of the diesel fuel was spilled into a storm sewer drain near the company's fueling station. Eventually, the fuel was carried into a nearby creek or lake. Local postal employees noticed an unusual odor and a "sheen" on the water. Governmental agencies responded, and eventually traced the "spill" to the incident. After defendant was convicted of littering, the appellate court found that the trial court did not err in construing "waste" to include diesel fuel, notwithstanding the lack of its particularized inclusion in the various statutory lists.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State ex rel. Ohio Cong. of Parents & Teachers v. State Bd. of Educ.


No. 2004-1668


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


111 Ohio St. 3d 568; 2006-Ohio-5512; 857 N.E.2d 1148; 2006 Ohio LEXIS 3066


November 29, 2005, Submitted  
October 25, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Blount-Hill v. Zelman, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 2932 (6th Cir.) (6th Cir. Ohio, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL AND CROSS-APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County, No. 03AP-508, 2004 Ohio 4421.
State ex rel. Ohio Cong. of Parents & Teachers v. State Bd. of Educ., 2004 Ohio 4421, 2004 Ohio App. LEXIS 4009 (Ohio Ct. App., Franklin County, Aug. 24, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, teacher, parent, and educational organizations, unions, and associations, and others, and appellees, state educational entities, community schools (CSs), and CS operators and managers, sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals for Franklin County (Ohio), which upheld the constitutionality of the CS system under R.C. ch. 3314 on only one claim and remanded other constitutional challenges for further determination.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged various aspects of R.C. ch. 3314 in their action, seeking declaratory, injunctive, and mandamus relief. The matter was bifurcated and only arguments addressing constitutional issues were determined. The trial court found that all of the constitutional claims asserted by appellants lacked merit and entered orders on pending motions accordingly. The court of appeals only agreed with the trial court's disposition regarding no impingement of Ohio Const. art. VI, § 3. There was no violation of local citizens' rights of local educational self-determination by use of local tax dollars for CSs under Ohio Const. art. VI, § 3 and Ohio Const. art. XII, § 5.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals with respect to the finding of constitutionality of CSs under Ohio Const. art. VI, § 3. The court reversed the court of appeals' decision to remand the remaining constitutional issues, and determined that R.C. ch. 3314 was constitutional as applied and on its face.

State v. Kapsouris


CASE NO. 06 MA 47


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, MAHONING COUNTY


2006-Ohio-7056; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 6990



December 18, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. Kapsouris, 2008 Ohio 1534, 2008 Ohio App. LEXIS 1366 (Ohio Ct. App., Mahoning County, Mar. 17, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Criminal Appeal from the Court of Common Pleas of Mahoning County, Ohio. Case No. 02 CR 395A.
State v. Kapsouris, 2005 Ohio 4476, 2005 Ohio App. LEXIS 4042 (Ohio Ct. App., Mahoning County, Aug. 23, 2005)



DISPOSITION: Sentence Vacated. Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Mahoning County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which, upon his convictions for aggravated robbery and felonious assault, imposed maximum and consecutive sentences on defendant. Defendant contended that his sentence, which was based on facts not found by a jury nor admitted by defendant, violated his Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: On appeal from the trial court's judgment, the court noted that, three days before the trial court's judgment entry was file stamped, the Ohio Supreme Court held in State v. Foster that various provisions in Ohio's felony sentencing statutes violated the Sixth Amendment because the trial judge, not the jury, was required to make certain findings to impose any sentence above the minimum sentence set forth in the statute. The State conceded that the trial court's judgment entry of sentence violated the principles set forth in Foster and agreed that the case should be remanded for resentencing. The court rejected defendant's contention that the trial court could only impose, at most, a three-year prison term based on due process and ex post facto concerns. Since the trial court had not had an opportunity to resentence defendant in light of the analysis and holding of Foster, any appeal challenging the penalty imposed upon resentencing had to wait until the trial court actually imposed the sentence.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's sentence and remanded the cause for resentencing under Foster.

State v. Berry


Case No. 05-CAA-06-0034


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DELAWARE COUNTY


2006-Ohio-1575; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 1468



March 30, 2006, Date of Judgment Entry


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. Berry, 2007 Ohio 4242, 2007 Ohio App. LEXIS 3927 (Ohio Ct. App., Delaware County, Aug. 14, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Criminal appeal from the Delaware County Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 04-CR-I-12-569.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Delaware County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) convicting defendant of theft in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2913.02(A)(1) and burglary, sentencing defendant to consecutive terms of four years and 17 months, and requiring defendant to serve the balance of time remaining on post-release control arising from a prior offense consecutively to the term imposed in the instant case.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that the trial court erred in sentencing defendant to a non-minimum prison term and to consecutive terms based on facts not found by the jury or admitted by defendant. Under a high court decision, Ohio Rev. Code Ann. §§ 2929.14(B), (C), (D)(2), (3), (E)(4), .19(B)(2), .41(A) were severed from the Ohio sentencing statutes. The excised portions removed only the presumptive and judicial findings that related to the findings necessary to increase the potential prison penalty. Those provisions were found unconstitutional under the Sixth Amendment. In the instant case, the sentence was based on these unconstitutional statutes. Thus, in accordance with the high court decision, a remand for a new sentencing hearing was required.


OUTCOME: The judgment was vacated. The case was remanded to the trial court for resentencing in accordance with the law and consistent with the appellate court's opinion.

City of Youngstown v. State


CASE NO. 07-MA-223


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, MAHONING COUNTY


2009-Ohio-5679; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 4800



September 9, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Civil Appeal from Court of Common Pleas of Mahoning County, Ohio. Case No. 06CV1677.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city filed suit against appellee, the State of Ohio, in the Court of Common Pleas of Mahoning County (Ohio), for a declaration that R.C. 9.481 was unconstitutional. The court found that the statute was constitutional. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: The city argued that R.C. 9.481 violated the Home Rule Provision, Uniformity Clause, and Contract Clause of the Ohio Constitution. On appeal, the court found that R.C. 9.481 was constitutional under the Ohio General Welfare Clause, Ohio Const. art. II, § 34, and the Ohio Home Rule Provision, Ohio Const. art. XVIII, § 3. Therefore, municipalities could not require their employees to reside in a particular municipality. Further, because R.C. 9.481 was a law of general nature and operated uniformly throughout the State of Ohio, it was constitutional under the Ohio Uniformity Clause, Ohio Const. art. II, § 26. Furthermore, the city, as a political subdivision created by the Ohio General Assembly, could not assert any constitutional protections regarding due course of law, under Ohio Const. art. I, § 16, or due process of law, under the Fourteenth Amendment, against the State, its creator. Finally, R.C. 9.481 did not violate the Ohio Contracts Clause, Ohio Const. art. II, § 28.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Duncan


APPEAL NO. C-990582


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, HAMILTON COUNTY


2000 Ohio App. LEXIS 1641



April 14, 2000, Date of Judgment Entry on Appeal


NOTICE:

 [*1] THESE ARE NOT OFFICIAL HEADNOTES OR SYLLABI AND ARE NEITHER APPROVED IN ADVANCE NOR ENDORSED BY THE COURT. PLEASE REVIEW THE CASE IN FULL.


PRIOR HISTORY: Criminal Appeal From: Hamilton County Court of Common Pleas. TRIAL NO. B-9903662.



DISPOSITION: Judgment Appealed From Is: Reversed and Appellant is Discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged a judgment of the Hamilton County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which convicted him of escape in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2921.34(A), alleging that the trial court erred in overruling his motion to dismiss the charge.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was released from prison after serving a sentence for corruption of a minor, and he was placed on post-release control pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2967.28. Appellant violated the conditions set forth by the parole authority. Consequently, appellant was ordered by the parole authority to complete an 18-month program at a volunteer facility. Appellant failed to comply with the program's requirements and, as a result, he was charged with escape. Thereafter, appellant filed a motion to dismiss the escape charge, arguing § 2967.28 was unconstitutional because it violated the constitutional doctrines of separation of powers and due process. Motion was denied. Appellant was found guilty of escape. Judgment reversed. Section 2967.28 was unconstitutional under the separation-of-powers doctrine because it assigned quasi-judicial functions to the parole authority, a state agency. As appellant was not under "detention," he did not, as a matter of law, violate the escape statute.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed. The state parole authority did not have the constitutional authority to perform the quasi-judicial functions of determining the duration of appellant's post-release control, setting the standard of behavior for appellant, determining when there had been a violation of set standard, and applying punishment for violation without judicial intervention.

State v. Bruhn


C.A. CASE NO. 17688


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, MONTGOMERY COUNTY


2000 Ohio App. LEXIS 199



January 28, 2000, Rendered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] T.C. CASE NO. 98 CR 3664.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant filed an appeal from a judgment of the Montgomery County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which classified him as a sexual predator.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was indicted for 36 counts of corruption of a minor. The indictment alleged that appellant had engaged in sexual conduct with his girlfriend's 13-year-old daughter. Pursuant to a plea agreement, appellant pleaded guilty to six of those counts. The trial court concluded that appellant was a sexual predator, pursuant to Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2950.01(E). Also, because persons of ordinary intelligence were not left to guess at the meaning of the term sexual predator, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. After reviewing the record, including the forensic evaluation, the appellate court concluded that there was clear and convincing evidence to support the trial court's determination that appellant was a sexual predator.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed, since the sexual predator statute was narrowly tailored, the statute was not vague or overbroad, and the record on appeal supported the trial court's finding that appellant was a sexual predator.

October 2, 2006, Date of Judgment Entry

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDINGS: Criminal Appeal from Common Pleas Court.
State v. McGhee, 2006 Ohio 1885, 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 1718 (Ohio Ct. App., Shelby County, Apr. 17, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Shelby County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which convicted him of engaging in a pattern of corrupt activity, in violation of R.C. § 2923.32. In a remand for resentencing, the trial court imposed a sentence of 10 years' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted and pursuant to a plea agreement, he entered a guilty plea to one charge in exchange for the State's dismissal of the other charges. His plea indicated that he understood the maximum term that could be imposed was 10 years' incarceration. The trial court sentenced defendant to the maximum term, which sentence was reversed and vacated on appeal under the authority of Foster. Upon remand for resentencing, a 10-year term was again imposed. On appeal, the court found that application of Foster did not violate federal notions of due process as established in Bouie and Rogers. Defendant was aware of the potential statutory sentence he faced, as he had notice that the Ohio sentencing statutes were subject to judicial scrutiny and he was unlikely to change his conduct in light of a sentencing change, given that his criminal conduct extended for over a year. The Foster holding did not create a substantively retroactive law and accordingly, it was not unconstitutional under Ohio Const. art. II, § 28. As presumed sentences could be taken away, defendant had no vested right that was affected by Foster. Additionally, no accrued substantial right was affected.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Pickett


APPEAL NO. C-000424


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, HAMILTON COUNTY


2001-Ohio-4022; 2001 Ohio App. LEXIS 5549



December 14, 2001, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Criminal Appeal From: Hamilton County Court of Common Pleas. TRIAL NO. B-9905091.



DISPOSITION: Trial court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury of murder, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.02(B) , and felonious assault, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.11(A)(1). Following the entry of judgment by the Hamilton County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the felony-murder rule, claiming the State had to prove a collateral felony, independent of the lethal act, to serve as the predicate offense for felony murder. In rejecting this argument, the appellate court found the State had proven the culpable-mental-state element by proving the intent of the predicate felony of felonious assault. Therefore, defendant was not denied due process. Defendant's challenge of the sufficiency and weight of the evidence was also rejected, as the State presented ample evidence that the victim's death was caused by trauma from at least three severe blows to her head, and not from a fall to the ground as defendant claimed. Defendant also claimed error in the rejection of certain of his self-defense evidence, in that the victim told a nurse that she planned to "act crazy with the boys on the street" so they would kill her. Although finding that two of the three reasons given by the trial court for rejecting the evidence were erroneous, the third reason, that the victim's statement was nearly two years old and was too far removed in time, provided an independent and proper basis for excluding the evidence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

550 of 997 DOCUMENTS


City of Riverside v. State


No. 10AP-126


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


190 Ohio App. 3d 765; 2010-Ohio-5868; 944 N.E.2d 281; 2010 Ohio App. LEXIS 4937



December 2, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by Riverside v. State, 128 Ohio St. 3d 1447, 2011 Ohio 1618, 2011 Ohio LEXIS 899 (2011)
Related proceeding at City of Riverside v. State, 2014-Ohio-1974, 2014 Ohio App. LEXIS 1925 (Ohio Ct. App., Montgomery County, May 9, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL from the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas. (C.P.C. No. 08CVH-08-12152).



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee city sued appellant State of Ohio and sought a declaratory judgment that R.C. 718.01(H)(11) was unconstitutional because it was preempted by 4 U.S.C.S. § 105 et seq., violated Ohio's one-subject rule, and violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the state and federal constitutions. The Franklin County Court of Common Pleas, Ohio entered summary judgment for the city and declared R.C. 718.01(H)(11) unconstitutional. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court's judgment fell solidly within R.C. 2505.02 since the trial court's judgment, declaring R.C. 718.01(H)(11) unconstitutional, unquestionably affected a substantial right. By declaring R.C. 718.01(H)(11) unconstitutional based on the city's preemption and one-subject rule theories, the trial court determined the city's claim for a declaratory judgment in its entirety. Therefore, Civ. R. 54(B) was inapplicable, and the judgment was a final, appealable order. The Buck Act, 4 U.S.C.S. § 105 et seq., extended to state and local taxing authorities jurisdiction to levy taxes in a federal area within its borders as though the area was not a federal area. The State's authority, including the authority to limit the municipalities' taxing authority, remained the same whether the taxes concerned income earned within or outside a federal enclave. Because the State may constitutionally limit the taxing authority of municipalities outside of federal enclaves, it must retain that authority within federal enclaves under the second sentence of 4 U.S.C.S. 106(a). Therefore, because there was no conflict between the Buck Act and R.C. 718.01(H)(11), R.C. 718.01(H)(11) was not preempted.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the decision and the law.

 

551 of 997 DOCUMENTS


Wetland Pres. Ltd v. Corlett


CASE NO. 2011-A-0034


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, ELEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, ASHTABULA COUNTY


2012-Ohio-3884; 975 N.E.2d 1033; 2012 Ohio App. LEXIS 3431



August 27, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Administrative Appeal from the Ashtabula County Court of Common Pleas, Case No. 2009 CV 1027.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a county auditor and a county board of revision, challenged a judgment of the Ashtabula County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which found that appellee wetland preservation company qualified for a reduced tax valuation under the "current agricultural use valuation" statute, R.C. 5713.30.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in applying the statute as written to the company's wetland mitigation bank. The company's wetland mitigation bank met the definition of "land devoted exclusively to agricultural use" under R.C. 5713.30(A)(1). Pursuant to Ohio Const. art. II, § 36, the General Assembly defined "agriculture" at R.C. 5713.30(A)(1) to include land that qualified for payment in a conservation program under an agreement with a federal agency, such as wetland mitigation banks. Since the Ohio Constitution did not define the term "agriculture," and the statute defined it to include wetland mitigation banks, there was no clear conflict between R.C. 5713.30(A)(1) and the Ohio Constitution. As a result, R.C. 5713.30(A)(1) was constitutional. The auditor failed to demonstrate that R.C. 5713.30(A)(1) was subject to interpretation. That statute was not ambiguous The express language of R.C. 5713.30(A)(1) clearly and unambiguously defined "land devoted exclusively to agricultural use" to include land that qualified for payment under a land conservation program under an agreement with a federal agency.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgment.

Rivera v. Overman


NO. 78013


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


2001 Ohio App. LEXIS 3012



July 5, 2001, Date of Announcement of Decision


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CHARACTER OF PROCEEDING: Civil appeal from Common Pleas Court, Case No. CV-391305.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured party sought review of a judgment from the Common Pleas Court (Ohio) that found in favor of the appellee driver.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the injured party argued that the version of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4513.263 in effect at the time of the accident prohibited the finder of fact from hearing evidence regarding whether or not the injured party was wearing properly adjusted seatbelts. The pre-Tort Reform Act version of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4513.263 prohibited the fact finder from hearing evidence on the issue of whether or not an injured party had been wearing a seatbelt. The Tort Reform Act, H.B. 350 (Ohio 1997) amended Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4513.263(F) to permit the finder of fact to hear evidence on the seatbelt issue. Subsequently, H.B. 215 (Ohio 1997) was enacted and used the identical language in Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4513.26(F) as had been used in the Tort Reform Act. Applying Stevens and the pre-Tort Reform version of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 4513.26(F) to the matter, it was clear that the trial court should not have permitted the jury to hear evidence that the injured party failed to wear her seatbelt.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded.

 

553 of 997 DOCUMENTS


Wilson v. AC&S, Inc.


CASE NO. CA2006-03-056


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TWELFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, BUTLER COUNTY


169 Ohio App. 3d 720; 2006-Ohio-6704; 864 N.E.2d 682; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 6580



December 18, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Wilson v. AC&S, Inc., 112 Ohio St. 3d 1475, 2007 Ohio 544, 861 N.E.2d 544, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 321 (2007)
Later proceeding at Wilson v. AC&S, Inc., 113 Ohio St. 3d 1437, 2007 Ohio 1374, 863 N.E.2d 655, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 676 (2007)
Application granted by, Cause dismissed by Wilson v. AC&S, Inc., 113 Ohio St. 3d 1457, 2007 Ohio 1787, 864 N.E.2d 645, 2007 Ohio LEXIS 847 (Ohio, Apr. 17, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: CIVIL APPEAL FROM BUTLER COUNTY COURT OF COMMON PLEAS. Case No. CV2001-12-3029.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee widow continued a personal injury suit filed by her deceased husband in the Butler County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) against appellants, producers, sellers, and distributors of asbestos. The trial court found that the widow's claim was governed by the law as it existed prior to the effective date of Am. Sub. H.B. 292, Gen. Assem. (Ohio, 2004), and appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found retroactive application of Am. Sub. H.B. 292, Gen. Assem. (Ohio, 2004) (H.B. 292), violated Ohio Const. art. II, § 28. The appellate court held such application did not unconstitutionally deprive the widow of a vested right, as she only had an expected future benefit based on the law's anticipated continuance, and no vested right existed in common law rules. R.C. § 2307.91(FF)'s definition, enacted by H.B. 292, of "substantial contributing factor" did not conflict with the formerly applied "substantial factor" test, so no substantive right was impaired. R.C. § 2307.91(FF)'s requirement that "competent medical authority" find no injury would have occurred absent asbestos exposure did not conflict with the "substantial factor" test as defining "competent medical authority," previously applied in such cases, was procedural, and its "but for" component was consistent with proximate cause's definition and the "substantial factor" test. H.B. 292 addressed collective asbestos exposure, but the "substantial factor" test addressed exposure to one product. H.B. 292's provisions were retroactively applicable remedial and curative statutes clarifying ambiguous phrases.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded.

State v. Owens


C.A. CASE NO. 23820


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, MONTGOMERY COUNTY


2010-Ohio-4923; 2010 Ohio App. LEXIS 4161



October 8, 2010, Rendered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
(Criminal appeal from Common Pleas Court). T.C. NO. 09CR644.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Montgomery County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which convicted him of failure to verify, in violation of R.C. 2950.06(A) and (F). Defendant had been reclassified as a Tier III sex offender pursuant to S.B. 10, Gen. Assem. (Ohio 2007).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was previously convicted of four rapes and one count of gross sexual imposition. He was sentenced to imprisonment and classified as a sexually oriented offender, such that he was required to verify his registration information annually for a 10-year period. Thereafter, defendant was reclassified as a Tier III sex offender and he was required to register every 90 days for life. Defendant was subsequently indicted for failing to verify his address with the county sheriff's office. After his dismissal motion was denied, defendant entered a no contest plea and was sentenced to imprisonment. On appeal, the court found that based on prior case precedent, R.C. 2950.031 and 2950.032 had been deemed to violate the separation of powers doctrine. They were previously excised from the statutory scheme. Accordingly, defendant's reclassification as a Tier III sex offender by the Attorney General was unconstitutional. Further, his conviction for failure to verify his address every 90 days pursuant to the severed statutes could not be enforced. Thus, it was proper to reinstate the classification and registration order originally imposed on defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and vacated defendant's conviction for failure to verify. It reinstated his original classification as a sexually oriented offender.

DeRolph v. State


No. 99-570


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


93 Ohio St. 3d 309; 2001-Ohio-1343; 754 N.E.2d 1184; 2001 Ohio LEXIS 2190


June 20, 2001, Submitted  
September 6, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 3, 2001. Order on Motion for Reconsideration of November 16, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Ohio LEXIS 3014. As Amended May 7, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Common Pleas Court of Perry County, No. 22043.



DISPOSITION: Ordered to implement changes described above. We have no reason to doubt defendants' good faith, we have concluded that there is no reason to retain jurisdiction of the matter before us. If order receives less than full compliance, interested parties have remedies available to them.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an exercise of continuing jurisdiction over the school funding dispute between appellees, individual and others, and appellants, state and others, the state supreme court was required to evaluate the constitutionality of the new school funding plan set forth in Am. Sub. H.B. 94 (Ohio 2001).


OVERVIEW: The state supreme court reviewed the new school funding plan in Am. Sub. H.B. 94 (Ohio 2001) and determined that, despite the extensive efforts of the state and the others to produce a plan that met the requirements previously announced by the state supreme court, changes to the formula were required to make the new plan constitutional. The state supreme court held, first, that the base cost formula, which recalculated the cost per student of providing an adequate education, had to be modified to include the top 5 percent districts and the lower 5 percent districts, and had to consider only those districts that actually met 20 of 27 performance standards without rounding. Second, the choice of the lower of fiscal year 1996 expenditures or fiscal year 1999 actual expenditures to calculate the base cost amount was unsupported by the evidence and should not have been used to lower the base cost amount figure. Third, the parity aid program, which attempted to provide poorer districts with funds similar to those available to wealthier districts, had to be fully funded no later than the beginning of fiscal year 2004, rather than fiscal year 2006.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court ordered the implementation of certain modifications to the new school funding plan so that the plan would meet the test for constitutionality that the state supreme court had previously set forth. The state supreme court relinquished jurisdiction of the case.

State v. Hardesty


Case No. 07CA2


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, PICKAWAY COUNTY


2007-Ohio-3889; 2007 Ohio App. LEXIS 3521



July 27, 2007, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by State v. Hardesty, 2008 Ohio 153, 2008 Ohio LEXIS 191 (Ohio, Jan. 23, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Hardesty, 2006 Ohio 5272, 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 5255 (Ohio Ct. App., Pickaway County, Aug. 11, 2006)


DISPOSITION:  [**1] JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the court vacated defendant's maximum sentence on the authority of Foster and remanded the matter for resentencing, the Pickaway County Common Pleas Court (Ohio) conducted a resentencing hearing and again imposed the same maximum sentence previously imposed. Defendant contended that the trial court violated both the Due Process Clause and the Ex Post Facto Clause of the U.S. Constitution by resentencing him to the maximum term in prison.


OVERVIEW: On appeal from the trial court's judgment resentencing defendant to the maximum five-year prison term upon his conviction for the third degree felony of sexual battery, the court noted that it had considered and rejected numerous times the same ex post facto and due process arguments raised by defendant. In affirming the trial court's judgment and overruling defendant's assignment of error, the court found that defendant had notice of the sentencing range at the time that he committed the offense. Foster neither judicially increased the range of his sentence nor retroactively applied a new statutory maximum to an earlier committed crime. Since the range of penalties for defendant's offense remained the same post-Foster as it was pre-Foster, the application of Foster's remedy did not violate his due process rights or act as an ex post facto application of the law. Further, the court noted that, as an Ohio appellate court, it was bound to follow the Supreme Court of Ohio's decision in Foster and could not overrule Foster or declare it unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Meeker v. Akron Health Dep't


C.A. No. 24539


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, NINTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, SUMMIT COUNTY


2009-Ohio-3560; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 3063



July 22, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by Meeker v. Akron Health Dep't, 2009 Ohio 6210, 2009 Ohio LEXIS 3391 (Ohio, Dec. 2, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM JUDGMENT ENTERED IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS COUNTY OF SUMMIT, OHIO. CASE No. CV 2007 07 4957.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a judgment from the Summit County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), which affirmed an administrative decision of appellee, a city public health department (PHD). The PHD determined that the owner had to abandon his septic system (SS) at his residence and connect to the city sewer system pursuant to Akron, Ohio, Codified Ordinance 50.02.


OVERVIEW: The owner's residence was built over 75 years ago, and it was equipped with a SS. Upon a prior inspection of the residence, the PHD determined that the SS was operable and that connection to the city sanitary sewer was not required at that time. Thereafter, the residence was sold to the owner. He was notified that he had to abandon his SS and connect to the city sewer system pursuant to § 50.02. The owner's administrative appeal was denied after a hearing, and the trial court also ruled in favor of the city. On further review, the court found no error in the trial court's determination that the city was not estopped from ordering the abandonment of the SS. The requirement that the owner connect to the city system was a governmental function. There was no indication in the statutory language or intent pursuant to R.C. 1.48 that § 50.02 was only to be applied prospectively, and the owner failed to show that compliance by him amounted to an impermissible retroactive application under Ohio Const. art. II, § 28. As R.C. 6117.51 only applied to certain county entities, the statutory exemption in § 6117.51(C) was properly deemed not applicable to the owner's system.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

City of Norton v. Richards


C. A. No. 24557


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, NINTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, SUMMIT COUNTY


2009-Ohio-3868; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 3288



August 5, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Discretionary appeal not allowed by Norton v. Richards, 2009 Ohio 6487, 2009 Ohio LEXIS 3526 (Ohio, Dec. 16, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM JUDGMENT ENTERED IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS COUNTY OF SUMMIT, OHIO. CASE No. CV 2008 11 8320.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee city sought declaratory and injunctive relief in the Summit County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio), alleging a sentence in a ballot issue adopted by voters was unconstitutional, under Ohio Const. art. II, § 28. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the city, declaring that the contested sentence was unconstitutionally retroactive. Appellant voter sought review.


OVERVIEW: The sentence held unconstitutional reduced a city council's at-large members from three to one by eliminating offices of members obtaining the second and third largest vote totals in an election held before the ballot issue was adopted. The appellate court held the sentence was retroactive because it reduced at-large city council members based on a prior election. Such a reduction could not rely on an event that occurred before deciding the reduction was needed. The sentence was substantive because, by seating only one of six candidates in one at-large position, it attached a new burden to a past transaction which held a reasonable expectation of finality. Voters sought to elect three candidates in the prior election with an expectation of finality. The sentence fundamentally changed the election's finality in that its effect was that voters chose only one candidate for one position. The sentence also disrupted certified election results showing three of six candidates being seated to three open positions by later selecting only one candidate to complete the term, attaching a new burden to a past transaction which held an expectation of finality for voters and candidates.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted on one count of murder, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.02. Defendant entered a plea of not guilty to the charge at his arraignment. Prior to trial, defendant entered a plea of guilty to an amended charge of involuntarily manslaughter, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2903.04(A). The trial court overruled defendant's motion, and defendant appealed. The appellate court found that it did not violate the doctrine of ex post facto or U.S. Const. amend. XIV guarantees of equal protection for a trial court to refuse to retroactively apply sentencing guidelines. As such, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed because it was not unconstitutional for the trial court to refuse to apply a sentencing guideline statute to defendant after the fact.

 

560 of 997 DOCUMENTS


161 Dublin, Inc. v. Ohio State Liquor Control Comm'n


No. 01AP-134


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, TENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, FRANKLIN COUNTY


2001-Ohio-8863; 2001 Ohio App. LEXIS 5905



December 27, 2001, Rendered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] APPEAL from the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment in favor of appellee was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant liquor permit holder had its liquor permit revoked by appellee liquor control commission, under Ohio Admin. Code § 4301:1-1-52(B), for improper conduct involving female dancers. On initial appeal, the revocation was affirmed by judgment of the Franklin County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio). The permit holder appealed the affirmance.


OVERVIEW: The liquor permit was revoked for engaging or allowing physical contact (touching, fondling, or caressing buttocks, female breasts, or self or patrons) to occur. The permit holder stipulated to the underlying facts, but challenged the constitutionality of the underlying administrative code. Since the specific section violations were not specified, the appellate court undertook an analysis and reviewed, for overbreadth and vagueness, the sections it found applicable. "Lewd activities," as used in Ohio Admin. Code § 4301:1-1-52(B)(1), was found to be substantially overbroad and unconstitutional, in that it incorporated a large amount of protected material. "Improper conduct of any kind," as used in Ohio Admin. Code § 4301:1-1-52(B)(7), was also unconstitutionally overbroad and vague. However, the provisions were found to be severable, and by striking the word "touch" from Ohio Admin. Code § 4301:1-1-52(B)(3), the rest of that subsection was found to be constitutional. It forbade the fondling or caressing of certain anatomical areas. Since the revocation was based upon this prohibited conduct, and that subsection was constitutional, the revocation was in accordance with the law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Boczek


CASE NOS. 2005-L-008 and 2005-L-009


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, ELEVENTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, LAKE COUNTY


2006-Ohio-3767; 2006 Ohio App. LEXIS 3739



July 21, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Criminal Appeals from the Court of Common Pleas, Case Nos. 04 CR 000370 and 04 CR 000491.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The victim, defendant's live-in girlfriend, was granted a five-year civil protection order against defendant. Subsequently, after two cases were joined, pursuant to Ohio R. Crim. P. 13, the Lake County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) convicted defendant of two counts of violating a protection order, in violation of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2919.27. He was sentenced to 12 months in prison on each count, to be served consecutively. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that his constitutional rights were violated when he was ordered to serve his sentences consecutively. The appellate court held that the issue was moot since defendant had completed his sentence. Further, the conviction was not against the manifest weight of the evidence. The protection order clearly prohibited defendant from making any contact, including telephone contact, with the victim. Because the victim's phone line was blocked from the jail, defendant got someone to place a three-way call. The victim's testimony that defendant called her cell phone, if believed, was sufficient to establish the violation of the protection order. Also, the victim's testimony was corroborated at the trial. Finally, defendant failed to rebut the presumption of constitutionality of Ohio Rev. Code Ann. § 2919.27 beyond a reasonable doubt. There was no due process issue. The evidence established that he received a copy and acknowledged receipt of the civil protection order approximately one week before the incident at issue. The record also showed that defendant was familiar with civil protection orders and had knowledge that he should not violate them.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Hall

State v. Bodyke


No. 2008-2502


SUPREME COURT OF OHIO


126 Ohio St. 3d 266; 2010-Ohio-2424; 933 N.E.2d 753; 2010 Ohio LEXIS 1271


November 4, 2009, Submitted 
June 3, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Bodyke, 126 Ohio St. 3d 1235, 2010 Ohio 3737, 933 N.E.2d 810, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 1933 (Ohio, Aug. 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:
APPEAL from the Court of Appeals for Huron County, Nos. H-07-040, H-07-041, and H-07-042, 2008 Ohio 6387.
State v. Bodyke, 2008 Ohio 6387, 2008 Ohio App. LEXIS 5330 (Ohio Ct. App., Huron County, Dec. 5, 2008)



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sex offenders sought review of the judgments of the Court of Appeals for Huron County (Ohio), which affirmed their sex offender reclassifications pursuant to the reclassification provisions in the Adam Walsh Act (AWA).


OVERVIEW: The offenders were classified initially under Megan's Law and reclassified under the AWA. The court held that stare decisis was not controlling in cases presenting a constitutional question; therefore, stare decisis did not compel the court to reach the same result that it had reached in past decisions. The court concluded that the reclassification scheme of the AWA, found in R.C. 2950.031 and R.C. 2950.032 and which required the attorney general to reclassify offenders who were previously classified by Ohio judges according to the provisions in Megan's Law and its precursor, violated the separation-of-powers doctrine by impermissibly instructing the executive branch to review past decisions of the judicial branch. Further, § 2950.031 and § 2950.032 violated the separation-of-powers doctrine by requiring the opening of final judgments, in that these statutes required the attorney general to reclassify sex offenders whose classifications had already been adjudicated by a court and made the subject of a final order. The court held that § 2950.031 and § 2950.032 were severed, and it reinstated the classifications and community-notification and registration orders previously imposed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the court of appeals. It severed § 2950.031 and § 2950.032, and it reinstated the classifications and community-notification and registration orders imposed previously by judges.

City of Cleveland v. State


No. 92663


COURT OF APPEALS OF OHIO, EIGHTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, CUYAHOGA COUNTY


185 Ohio App. 3d 59; 2009-Ohio-5968; 923 N.E.2d 183; 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 5017



November 12, 2009, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at City of Cleveland v. State, 2009 Ohio 6106, 2009 Ohio App. LEXIS 5135 (Ohio Ct. App., Cuyahoga County, Nov. 19, 2009)
Appeal granted by Cleveland v. State, 124 Ohio St. 3d 1505, 2010 Ohio 799, 922 N.E.2d 969, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 611 (2010)
Motion granted by Cleveland v. State, 125 Ohio St. 3d 1460, 2010 Ohio 2753, 928 N.E.2d 736, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 1446 (2010)
Motion granted by Cleveland v. State, 126 Ohio St. 3d 1541, 2010 Ohio 3855, 932 N.E.2d 336, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 1997 (2010)
Reversed by, Remanded by City of Cleveland v. State, 2010 Ohio 6318, 2010 Ohio LEXIS 3322 (Ohio, Dec. 29, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:
Civil Appeal from the Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas. Case No. CV-618492.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city filed a complaint for declaratory judgment challenging the constitutionality of R.C. 9.68. Two gun organizations moved to intervene as defendants and to bring cross-claims against the city alleging that local firearm ordinances were unconstitutional. The motions were denied. The Cuyahoga County Court of Common Pleas (Ohio) granted appellee State's motion for summary judgment. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: The City argued that the trial court erred in denying its motion for summary judgment because R.C. 9.68 violated Ohio Const. art. XVIII, § 3, the Home Rule Amendment. The appellate court held that the trial court erred by denying the city's motion for summary judgment. Because R.C. 9.68 failed to satisfy the general law test, it was not a general law. Therefore, R.C. 9.68 unconstitutionally attempted to limit municipalities' home-rule police powers. Under the Canton test, R.C. 9.68 was not part of a statewide and comprehensive legislative enactment. Although Sub. H.B. 347, Gen. Assem. (Ohio) pertained to a matter of statewide concern, it was not comprehensive and left a great deal of firearm activity unregulated. Further, with R.C. 9.68, the State attempted to curtail the city's home-rule police powers without enacting legislation to remedy the purported ill of a confusing "patchwork" of municipal regulations involving firearms. Finally, R.C. 9.68 did not prescribe a rule of conduct upon citizens generally. Additionally, R.C. 9.68 violated the separation-of-powers doctrine espoused by the Ohio Constitution in Ohio Const. art. II, § 32.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the cause was remanded with directions for the trial court to enter summary judgment for the city.
